6.10       During loan negotiations the following agreements were reached
with the Central Bank on:
(a) the ceiling of US$6.0 million on subloans under the project (para.
4.02 and 6.06);
/1 Approximately the same spread presently accrues to PDCP and PISO on
existing Bank loans. An attractive margin for PFIs, particularly the
commercial banks, is essential to encourage them to lend long-term.
/2  This was the rate charged by DBP on the latest Bank loan (1572-PH).  DBP
raised its general interest rate to 16-18% in 1980 after the Bank loan
had been almost fully committed.
/3 Service fee is charged by the Central Bank at this rate on funds with
floating interest rate feature that are being presently relent to
financial institutions under the its Consolidated Foreign Borrowing
Program.



- 53 -
(b) the procedures relating to accreditation, evaluation and supervision
of PFIs and the Bank's participation in these matters (paras. 4.16
and 4.19);
(c) the disbursement procedures applicable to the proceeds of
the proposed loan and other resources of ADFU, (para. 4.23);
(d) the amount and timing of co-financing (para. 4.25);
(e) the relending terms from the Central Bank to PFIs and from
PFIs to the end-users (paras 6.04, 6.08 and 6.09); and
(f) the Central Bank's "free limit" of US$4 million on sub-
loans (para. 6.05).
6.11      In addition, during negotiations the Bank reached the following
understandings with the Central Bank on:
(a) the internal organization, staffing, and training programs
of ADFU (para. 4.11-4.14);
(b) the number of PFIs to be accredited (para. 4.18);
(c) guidelines on procurement for ADFU and PFIs (para. 4.22);
(d) the issues relating to PDCP (para. 5.06, 5.10 and 5.11) and PISO
(para. 5.14, 5.16, 5.19 and 5.23);
(e) specific measures to be taken to improve DBP's operations and
institutional set-up as spelled out in a "Program of Action"
(para. 5.28); and
(f) the accumulation of the Central Bank's service fee in excess of
3/4 of 1% and its use for project purposes (para. 6.09).
Recommendation
6.12      A loan of US$150 million with a maturity of 20 years, including a
grace period of 5 years, is recommended, for the Central Bank of the
Philippines.

PHILIPPINES
INDUSTRIAL FINANCE PROJECT
Philippine Government's Statement of Policy on the
Future Evolution of the Financial Sector
AiniofrLO F finanl
13 March 1981
Mr. PRbert McNamara
President
International Bank for Reconstruction and Develcprent
1818 H Street
Washington, D.C., U. S. A.
Dear Mr. YrkNamara:
In connection with the loan application for relending to the private
sector, I wish to outline the financial sector policies which the Philip-
pines is pursuing.
Financial Sector Policy
1.  The Philippines is undertaking major reform  in its financial
system with a view to making it more respcnsive to the imperatives of
the next stages of eccnomic development. With increasing modernization
of the agricultural sector and the need to restructure industrial gr,7th
to operate in an internatimally more competitive environment, there
will be a growing demand for savings and for effective ways of c-armneling
such savings to priority investments. This will require not only a
higher rate of savings but also a need that a greater proportion of these
savings be in financial form and intermediated through the financial sys-
tem. Furthenrmore the availability of and access to longer term funds
will have to be increased.  Ihe efficiency of the system will also have
to be improved through increased cacpetition and possiblities of eccnomies
of scale. These are the underlying objectives tboards which changes are
being directed in the financial system.
2.  Ihe reforms are well advanced and cooTbine elements of evolution and
innovation. The reforms are addressed to both the suppliers' and users'
sides of the financial system and therefore conprehend the whole pro-
cess of financial intermediation.
3.  The key thrust is flexibility.  Institutionally, financial inter-
mediaries are being made more flexible by reducing the differentiation
among the various categories and broadening their powers. For exarJpl.e,
certain banks may now go into equity instead of confining themselves to



ANNEX 1
55                                     Page 2
lending. In monetary policy, the Monetary Board is exercising the
flexible powers newly conferred on it by law to simplify the interest
rate structure and make it more market-oriented. Savers can expect a
better return on their savings, while users must be prepared to bear
the cost of funds better reflecting the realities of a world infla-
tionary environment. Both savers and users are being given incentives
to go into long-term transactions.
4.      The authorities, however, are cognizant of the need to be prag-
matic and believe that a rigid adherence to strict models is bound to
create problems. The expansion of powers of certain categories of
financial institutions does not operate automatically; it is dependent
on a level of entreprenuerial skill, managerial expertise and resource
adequacy which must be monitored and encouraged by the authorities.
Likewise, the trend away from specialization does not absolve us from
the need for devising special programs for critical areas like agri-
culture and housing.
5.      Furthermore, unrestrained market forces solely determining levels
of interest rates are hard to justify at this stage in view of the fact
that the financial system is more of an oligopolistic system than a per-
fectly competitive one. Thus, the case for greater market orientation
has to be approached gradually and tempered by the need for regulatory
and indicative magnitudes which would serve as fulcrums around which the
system would function.
6.      The basis for the changes adopted in 1980 was set out in seven
laws, which amended the legislative framework of the banking system.
The legiLlative changes have been supported by appropriate revision of
banking regulations, changes in monetary policy and procedures and
amendments in tax laws to give needed fiscal incentives for achievement
of the objectives of the reforms.
7.      The heart of the reform consists of a revision of the banking
structure and has the following main features:
(a) The introduction of a new type of institution-
"texpanded commercial banks"- which would have
statutory ability to perform both commercial
and investment banking functions, including
investments in equities.
(b) A reduction in differentiation among the
various categories of financial institutions
by permitting thrift banks full domestic
banking powers and by changing the unit
concept of rural banks through regional
association of federations, or through
mergers and consolidations to branch banking
and to give them ability to mobilize larger
resources and service larger clients and to
diversify their activities.



ANNEX 1
Page 3
- 56 -
(c)  An increase in the powers of nonbank financial
intermediaries (NBFIs) and the grant to them
of access to Central Bank credit facilities.
(d)  The institutional structure has been made
flexible and the Monetary Board has been
given discretionary powers to permit one
category of institution to evolve into a
higher category.
8.      Subject to compliance with eligibility criteria, to be estab-
lished from time to time by the Monetary Board, commercial banks and
investment houses can apply to become "expanded" banks and thrift and
rural banks can seek to become commercial banks. Generally speaking,
the Monetary Board will permit movement from one category to another
on the basis of financial resources, technical and professional
capability and demonstrated past performance. Instead of the cate-
gorized specialization of the past, the functional growth of financial
institutions has now been made an evolutionary process, controlled by
discretionary powers vested with the Monetary Board.
9.      Besides the objectives of reducing categorized specialization
to bring about greater competition as between various types of insti-
tutions and to make their operations more efficient, one primary ob-
jective of the banking reforms is to lengthen the maturity of lending.
Accordingly, the following measures have been introduced:
(a)  The Government has adopted a flexible interest
rate policy and ha-- moved toward deregulation
of interest rates. Ceilings on loans of
maturities in excess of 730 days have been
abolished. Banks have also been permitted
to charge variable interest rates on their
long-term loans to protect lending margins
in situations where there is a rise in re-
placement cost of short-term funds used for
term lending. Likewise, to assist in
mobilization of long-term funds, interest
rate ceilings on all types of deposits have
been abolished, except on demand deposits
for which no interest is paid. However,
loans of up to 730 days' maturity will still
be subject to ceilings of 18% for chattel
loans and 16% for loans secured by land, both
of which already incorporate the 2% service
fee.
(b)  Reserve requirements agaYinst most deposit
funds are to be reduced.16% and made uni-
form for all financial intermediaries
authorized to accept such deposits. The
reduction will mitigate the cost pressures
on lending rates while standardization
shall place lending



ANNEX 1
Page 4
- 57 -
institutions on a more equal footing to
enhance competition. Undesirable excesses
in liquidity that may result from such re-
ductions will be controlled by appropriate
measures like open market operations.
Conversely, a 5% reserve requirement shall
be imposed on long-term deposits (over
730 days) that will provide both a monitor
and control mechanism on the growth of such
deposits. The magnitude of such a require-
ment is deemed to have only a marginal effect
on long-term lending rates.
(c) Rediscounting facilities of the Central Bank
to extend perferential credit to priority
activities have been further simplified. Re-
discount value of loans were narrowed down to
two ranges: namely 100% and 80%. Rediscount
rate and the corresponding ceilings on lending
rates have also been reduced resulting in three
basic spreads of 3, 6 and 9 per cent between
the rediscount rates ana the lending rates.
(d) A lender-of-last resort facility on a consistent
and essentially automatic basis has been estab-
lished by the Central Bank to enable banks to
tide over temporary liquidity problems that
might arise as a result of term transformation
or other factors.
(e) The Government has provided a number of fiscal
incentives to the commercial banks to become
itexpanded banks" and lengthen the maturity of
their lending. The gross recipts tax now pro-
gressively declines as loan maturities get longer
and there is no such tax on interest income on
loan maturities exceeding seven years. More-
over,dividend income of the "expanded banks" will
be exempt from corporate tax. Finally, the
capital gains arising from banks' mergers and
consolidation will also be exempt from tax.
(f) The Monetary Board has been given discretionary
powers to lower the equity to risk assets ratio
to provide increased leverage to banks to en-
able them to increase their long-term loan portfolio.



ANNEX 1
Page 5
_ 58 -
Provided the banks neet the necessary cnditions, their
risk assets ratio can be reduced fran 10% to 8% or even 6%.
10.  Ihe Governnent intends to mnmitor closely the performance of the
financial sector, and will ensure, through such additional measures as
may be necessary, that it fully and adequately supports and assists in
the realization of the objectives of Government's industrial policy.
Khereas Government will remain sensitive to ensuring that the financial
needs of develcpment are being fully met and would take necessary legis-
lative, regulatory and administrative actions, the following are sae
inportant specific cbjectives which the Government would seek to achieve
in the next tWDo years:
(a) lhe initial net worth eligibility of P500 million for
licensing of "expanded banks" reflects the cautious atti-
tude on the part of the Govemnent. Investment banking,
particularly equity financirg, requires a scphisticated
organization, professional capacity and financial strength.
On the other hand, Government is cognizant of the fact that
"expanded" authority in the hands of only a few large
instituticns may not result in sufficient cmpetition
and the needs of the smaller borrowers and new entrepre-
neurs may not be satisfied. It is, therefore, (bvernnent's
intention to make "expanded' banking more broad-based by
encouraging wider public ownership and conpetiticn among
a larger number of "expanded banks" either by hastening
the process of institutional mergers and consolidation so
that more institutimns become eligible and/or by lowering
the net worth qualification limit. Such action, however,
will be taken after Government has had sufficient opportu-
nity to evaluate the performance of expanded banks that
are now being licensed and after it has acquired sufficient
kno-how to monitor and guide their operations.
(b) The Government has already adopted measures under

of export industries and, in addition to fiscal
incentives, short-term special discounting facilities
are available in the Central Bank for export finance.
The Philippine Export and Foreign Loan Guarantee
Corporation has established a guarantee facility
for loans to exporters from commercial banks. In
addition, a Cottage Industry Guarantee Fund has
also been set up to support the financial require-
ments of the cottage industry that is predominantly
composed of small-scale producers and mainly oriented
towards exports. In the light of changing export
,structure, the need for a medium-term export finan-
cing was considered which resulted in the recent
creation of the Export Credit Corporation.

12.    The Gbvernment believes that realistic interest rates increase financial
savings and improve allocation of resources and, in principle, it favors positive
real interest rate in relation to inflation. Hcwever, complete deregulation of
interest rates in the Cbverunent's view is undesirable at this tine.  Rapid
changes in interest rates in response to short term fluctuations in the
inflationary situation could be destabilizing and result in an uncertain financial

climate. Besides, such change may be unnecessary, particularly when the
Cbverrment is adopting other neasures open to it to control inflation. An
excessively high rate of interest can cgirplicate efforts to contain inflaticn.
Ihere are transactions balances, such as demand deposits and even perhaps
savings deposits to scme extent, which are interest- inelastic and it would
be a misapplication of interest-rate policy to use it in a shotgun fashion.
Mbreover, the financial imarket is not a perfectly ccorpetitive one and market
rates may reflect the influence of oligopolistic eleaents. In the context
of these considerations, the Gbvernment is comnitted to the general princi-
ple of positive real interest rates, both on tine deposits and loans.
Positive real rates nest, hcwever, be based on definitive underlying infla-
tionary trends, as distinct fron short--term fluctuations in price indices
brought about by transitory or external factors beyond Cbwermnent's control.
In this regard, the Gbvernrent will establish suitable criteria and set up
apprcpriate mechanism for a regular review of the interest rate structure,
including interest paid on savings deposits and NOW accounts.

PHILIPPINES
INDUSTRIAL FINANCE PROJECT
Operating Policy Guidelines of the Apex Development Finance Unit
Central Bank of the Philippines
Framework
1.        This paper sets forth the objectives and basic principles of
policy and operating procedures of the Central Bank APEX Development
Finance Unit (ADFU). The policies and procedures will apply to all lending
operations of ADFU.
Objectives and Goals
2.        The dominant objective of ADFU is to assist in promoting and
financing sound industrial development in the Philippines, in line with
government policy and in coordination with other Central Bank offices and
other government offices/institutions by chanelling funds through suitable
financial institutions capable of evaluating sound investment proposals and
able to undertake efficient intermediation of medium- and long-term
financial resources. To achieve these objectives ADFU will perform, among
others, the following major functions:
(a) administer financial resources provided/loaned to the Central
Bank by international lending agencies and by other sources
acceptable to Central Bank for relending by ADFU to
participating financial institutions (PFIs) for financing the
medium- and long-term needs of the Philippine industrial
sector, all in accordance with national economic priorities;
(b) make continuing efforts, but subject to stringent tests of
admissibility, to enlarge the number of institutions receiving
ADFU's financial assistance with a view to making industrial
finance widely available and making improvements in the general
level of efficiency in term lending and investment banking
operations of Philippine financial institutions, particularly
in the matter of resource use and resource mobilization; and
(c) to provide guidance and assistance to PFIs for improving their
organization, intermediation efficiency, and project evaluation
capability.



- 63 -                          ANNEX 2
Page 2
Approval of PFIs
3.        ADFU financing will be available only to accredited financial
institutions. Accreditation of PFIs will be approved by the Monetary Board
of the Central Bank of the Philippines on recommendations by ADFU. The
prerequisite to accreditation of a PFI will be a detailed evaluation of the
applicant-institution, taking into account the following major criteria:
(a) sound financial position and performance as evidenced by
balance sheets and income statements and a healthy and
sound portfolio;
(b) sound operating policies and procedures and a well-articulated
plan and strategy for future operations which will ensure
a development orientation, continued viability and financial
strength of the institution;
(c) an organization basically sound in its structure and a management
and operating staff of undoubted competence and possessing all
requisite expertise to undertake technical, economic and
financial appraisal of projects and their supervision; and
(d) compliance with all relevant laws, decrees and regulations to
the satisfaction of all regulatory authorities.
ADFU may, where necessary, recommend to the Monetary Board to prescribe
conditions, consistent with Central Bank's existing rules and regulations,
with regard to capital structure, operating policies, strategy and planning
for future operations and organization and staffing which it would like to
see fulfilled before approving an institution as a PFI. ADFU may in
addition recommend to the Monetary Board to prescribe conditions, among
others, with regard to size of loans, exposure limits, gearing ratios and
dividend and reserve policy of PFIs, and terms and conditions governing
subloans, including interest rates and repayment terms. The appropriate
Central Bank Departments and/or Offices will be duly consulted by ADFU
while drawing up condition6 of accreditation.
4.        Government-owned financial institutions applying for accreditation
will be required to meet the following two additional conditions as a
prerequisite of their accreditation:
(a) support from the Government, in form and substance acceptable
to ADFU, that the applicant institution will be given financial
assistance against losses arising from undertaking of projects
which do not meet the normal banking criteria, but are considered
by the Government as economically and socially desirable, after
taking into account the overall financial viability and liquidity
position of such applicant institution; and



- 64 -
ANNEX  2
Page 3
(b)  a commitment by the applicant institution that its lending
terms (in particular, interest rates) would be generally
consistent with the interest rate policy of the Government
and with the objective of development of a competitive market-
oriented long-term lending market.
Use of ADFU Loans by PFIs
5.        ADFU loans to PFIs will be used to finance:  (a) economically and
commercially viable projects consistent with the Government's development
plans in the manufacturing industry, tourism (Government-approved tourist
facilities outside Metro Manila), mining, shipping and agro-industry; and
(b) capital assets, including but not limited to plant sites, factory
buildings, plant equipment and fixtures and their installation and transport
equipment. ADFU will not ordinarily permit its loan to be applied for
refinancing of existing debts.
Project Selection and Appraisal
6.        ADFU will not involve itself in selection and evaluation of
projects and this function will be exercised by PFIs in accordance with
established and agreed criteria. ADFU will, however, review project
appraisals prepared by PFIs and take a decision in accordance with the
provisions of paragraphs 7, 8 and 9 below.
Free Limit
7.        ADFU will establish a "free limit" for each participating
institution. Projects whose financing needs fall within the "free limit"
will not require a detailed review by ADFU and funds will be released after
ensuring that the documentation is complete and the terms and conditions of
the loan are in accordance with agreed criteria. Appraisals relating to
projects whose loan requirements exceed the "free limit" will be reviewed
in detail in accordance with provisions contained in paragraph 9.
8.         The "free limit" for each individual PFI will be determined by
ADFU and, where required, by agreement with ADFU's creditors. The free
limit may, from time to time, be increased or reduced depending upon the
performance of each PFI. In determining the "free limit" for PFI, ADFU
will take into account the maturity of the institution and its capacity to
identify, appraise and supervise projects.
Review by ADFU of Projects in Excess of Free Limit
9.        Appraisal reports of projects whose financial needs are higher
than the "free limit" will be submitted by the PFIs to ADFU for its review
and approval. The appraisal reports will provide all necessary data



- 65 -
ANNEx 2
Page 4
required to establish the technical, financial and economic feasibility of
the project.  ADFU may, on the basis of its review of the appraisal
reports, either approve a project for financing and authorize release of
funds for disbursement except in cases where a final approval of ADFU's
creditor is required, or seek additional data or suggest a new line of
evaluation or propose modification in the project design and concept or
reject the project as not suitable for financing.
Procurement Policy of ADFU
10.        ADFU will review, for all projects, whether above or below the
"free limit," the appropriateness of the procedure followed or proposed to
be followed by PFIs for procurement of goods and services necessary to
carry out the project, and may propose modifications if these procedures do
not conform to criteria or guidelines set out in the loan agreements with
PFIs.
Monitoring of Performance of Projects
11.       Each PFI will be responsible for monitoring and supervising the
implementation and operations of projects financed by it. ADFU, through
its monitoring of the PFIs, will ensure that the supervision function is
being effectively carried out and, in particular, that projects facing
problems are being constantly reviewed, their difficulties are being
analyzed, remedial measures are being prescribed and all necessary steps
are being taken to assist such projects in overcoming their problems while
at the same time, protecting the PFI's investment in the project. To
effectively perform the monitoring functions ADFU shall: (a) require the
PFIs to submit periodic reports in a prescribed form; and (b) send represen-
tatives to PFIs to inspect their financial, operational and project
supervision records.
Monitoring of PFIs
12.        ADFU will monitor the performance of PFIs to ensure that they
continue to operate in conformity with sound principles and are upgrading
the quality of their staff and their appraisal and analytical skills. ADFU
will undertake a performance evaluation of each PFI at least once each year.
Relationship with End-Users
13.        The lender-borrower relationship will exist between the Central
Bank and PFI and not between the Central Bank and the ultimate beneficiaries.
Contacts with project entities or visits to projects shall be made by ADFU
through the particilar PFI.



- 66 -
ANNEX  2
Page 5
Autonomous Status of ADFU
14.       Within the parameters of its duties and responsibilities as
defined by the Monetary Board and subject to such regulations as the
Monetary Board may from time-to-time prescribe, ADFU will be a unit
reporting directly to the Governor of the Central Bank and responsible to
him. In pursuing its objectives ADFU will act as an autonomous unit, but it
will operate within the broad scope of Central Bank's rules and regulations.
Staffing
15.       ADFU will adopt all necessary measures to ensure that it is
adequately staffed with qualified persons.  Recruitment will emphasize
professional skills and employment will ordinarily be on a full-time
regular basis, though in the initial stages, secondment of suitable Central
Bank staff on a temporary basis for specific assignments may be considered.
ADFU will provide training opportunities to its staff to upgrade their
skills.
Accounting and Auditing
16.       The Central Bank will maintain ADFU accounts in accordance with
generally accepted accounting principles and will have them audited by
appropriate qualified and competent auditors.
AEPID
April 1, 1981

Outline of ADFU's Guidelines on Procurements (draft)
1.        All subproject appraisal reports, regardless of project/loan size,
will contain a description of the procurement process foreseen (or actually
initiated, if that is the case), with a statement of why the procedure chosen
is the most appropriate. (ADFU may query any project in this respect, whether
within or above the free limit allowed to the PFI.)
2.        When the proposed subloan is above the PFI-s free limit, the
appraisal report must specifically consider/discuss ICB, and make a case,
among alternatives, for method chosen (whether ICB or other). ADFU must
obviously be satisfied before approving the subproject, and may query and
suggest reconsideration of the method chosen before approval.
3.        When the proposed subloan is above ADFU's free limit of $4 million,
the PFI will do basically the same thing, but with the presumption that ICB
is appropriate (i.e., the PFI must make a convincing case, if ICB is not
chosen, why it was appropriate not to do so). ADFU will add its endorsement
to the PFI report on this point before forwarding the project appraisal to
the Bank. After some experience has been acquired, the Bank and ADFIJ will
consult, from time to time, about the criteria used in reaching decisions,
and whether the overall trend would seem to be reasonable.
4.        Guildelines will be worked out, in the light of gradually acquired
experience, together with PFIs, as to what criteria to use in the considera-
tion of, not just ICB, but various procurement methods. The suggested
procedure above may be modified, as necessary, in consultation with the Bank.
5.        The Loan Agreement between the Bank and the Central Bank
and Subsidiary Loan Agreements between the Central Bank and PFIs will
stipulate that "the methods and procedures to be used for the procurement of
goods and services required for the Investment Project" will be included
among the items mandatory in the description of the Project.
6.        There will be included in the Subsidiary Agreements with the PFIs
following clause to reflect the responsibility of these institutions to induce
and monitor appropriate procurement methods for projects financed by them.
"For projects financed by [PFI], [PFIJ will endeavor
to ensure that acceptable practices are followed in
the procurement of goods and services necessary to



- 68 -
ANNEX 3
Page 2
carry out the project, with a view to enable a
reasonably broad domestic and international
selection and considered comparison of bids,
offers or quotations to ensure optimum quality
at the best possible price."
AEPID
March 3, 1981

PHILIPPINES
INDUSTRIAL FINANCE PROJECT
Private Development Corporation of the Philippines (PDCP):
A Review of the InstitLtional, Operational and Financial Aspects
Background, Objectives and Functions
1.        PDCP, one of the country s leading investment houses, was set up
in 1963 with the active support of the Bank and IFC./l. Its objectives are to
assist in the development of privately-owned industries, promote and
facilitate participation of private capital from local and foreign sources;
widen the ownership base of Filipino enterprises; and help promote industrial
development and managerial expertise in the country. To attain these
objectives, PDCP's Articles of Incorporation, inter alia, empower it to
undertake all necessary functions including extending loans, investing in
equity, issuing guarantees, underwriting public offerings and syndicating
loans. In the 17 years of its existence, PDCP has developed into a mature DFC
and has made a significant contribution towards the growth of the country-s
private industry.
The Economic Role of PDCP
2.        PDCP is next to DBP as the most important source of long-term
finance for industry in the country. Over the 1975-79 period, PDCP-assisted
projects accounted for 2.1% of the total capital formation of the country. As
a matter of policy, PDCP closely aligns its operations to the economic
priorities set by the Government, but it remains very selective in its choice
of projects, assisting only those which satisfy its criteria for financial,
managerial, technical, market and economic viability. PDCP assists both
large-scale and, to a lesser extent, small-scale industries, including
projects relating to mining, public utilities, transport and manufacturing.
3.        As of September 30, 1979, PDCP had assisted 860 projects with
loans and investments amounting P 2.6 billion. Including investments by
the project proponents and other institutions, total investment in these
projects amounted to about P 6.6 billion. Upon completion, these projects
are estimated to generate some P 9.6 billion in incremental annual sales,
of which P 3.4 billion would be export sales. The annual foreign exchange
earnings/savings of these projects net of import requirements, was estimated
to be P 2.7 billion. The gross value added of the projects financed by
PDCP was estimated to amount to P 5.7 billion, while net value added was
estimated at P 4.5 billion.
/1 Besides the Bank loan of $15 million, IFC participated in PDCP-s initial
capitalization through an equity investment of $210,000 (since sold).



ANNEX 4
- 70-                              Page 2
4.        Resource Allocation.  On the whole, resource allocation by PDCP
has been efficient. This is borne out by the fact that the financial rate
of return for a representative sample of 151 PDCP-assisted projects ranged
from 9% to 54% with an average of 25.2%; the range of economic rates of
return, for a sample of 73 projects, was 11% to 90%, with an average of
38.2%. Through its small business loans, PDCP maintains a balance in its
lending between SMI and large-scale industry. Small loan approvals have
increased rapidly from P 3.6 million in 1975 to P 33.2 million in 1979.
In addition to financial assistance, PDCP provides valuable technical and
managerial assistance on project formulation and implementation to small
entrepreneurs. PDCP-s assistance continues to be well-distributed
throughout the various industrial sectors and subsectors, with no undue
exposure in any single industry. PDCP also continues to place high priority
on projects located outside Metro Manila. To this end, PDCP has established
six branch offices and plans to open more in the future. Consequently, the
geographical distribution of PDCP's loans is more diversified than the typical
pattern of industrial investment in the Philippines.
5.        Employment Generation.  An important criterion used by PDCP for
project selection is the project's potential for generating new jobs. PDCP
does finance large capital-intensive projects but only after a careful
consideration of other economic merits of the project and relative priority
of the industry in the context of the country-s development. A primary
objective of launching of PDCP's small business financing program in 1972
was to assist in the establishment and development of labor-intensive
projects. As of September 30, 1979, PDCP-assisted projects are estimated to
have generated some 43,000 fiew jobs with an average cost per job of $20,800.
Average cost per job for small loans was about $5,200 while for regular loans
it was $27,500 in 1979.
6.        Resource Mobilization.  The country-s financial environment has, in
the past, seriously constrained PDCP-s ability to mobilize long-term resources
and as a result, PDCP has had to rely largely on official resources for funds.
About 85% of PDCP's long-term resources (P 2,273.5 million) as of March 31,
1980 were raised from official sources (the Bank, ADB, Commonwealth Develop-
ment Corporation (CDC), USAID and IGLF) (Annex 4, T - 1). Apart from its
equity funds (P 165.7 million), the resources mobilized by PDCP include peso
borrowings through the issuance of medium-term notes payable (P 63.4 mil-
lion), and foreign currency borrowings of $15 million from IFC and $10 million
from a consortium of banks headed by the Bank of Tokyo. Considering the
circumstances and constraints, PDCP's resource mobilization efforts have
achieved reasonable results. The country-s financial sector reforms of 1980
should improve PDCP-s ability to mobilize domestic resources in the future.
In addition to raising resources directly for its own use, PDCP indirectly
helps its clients in raising both domestic and foreign currency resources
through its local and international syndications; funds so far mobilized
through syndications amount to $34.7 million in foreign exchange and P 147.0
million in domestic currency.



ANNEX 4
- 71 -                          Page 3
7.        Other Developmental Activities.  PDCP also undertakes a number of
nonfinancial development activities. It performs and publishes selected
industry studies/market profiles which help entrepreneurs identify attractive
investment possibilities open to them; provides training in the field of
development finance not only for its own staff, but also for the staff of
other private and Government institutions in the Philippines and from abroad;
and organizes special conferences and industry dialogues to discuss major
industry problems.
Institutional Aspects
8.        Ownership, Management and Staff.  PDCP's ownership is distributed
70.3% among Filipino and 29.7% among foreign stockholders. The ownership is
widely distributed and there are nearly 1,300 stockholders. Among the
Filipino stockholders, the maximum concentration of holding with one group
(Trans Pacific Investment Corporation) is only 12.8%; the rest of the stocks
are held among a large number of unrelated groups and individuals. The
foreign stockholders are institutions from USA, UK, Japan, West Germany,
France and Switzerland.
9.        PDCP-s Board of Directors /1 has 11 members from diverse, but com-
plementary professional fields and who operate very effectively in overseeing
PDCP's management and operations. The Board sets policies and the general
directions, reviews PDCP-s actual performance and approves new financial
commitments. PDCP possesses an organization structure which is adequate for
achieving its objectives and has remained basically unchanged since 1976.
PDCP remains a dynamic institution and its Board and management remain
sensitive to the need for change should evolving circumstances so warrant.
Some changes in organization may be needed to deal with the challenges of a
more competitive business environment following the financial sector reforms,
to provide for more opportunities for internal staff promotions and to further
reduce the staff turnover rate (see para. 10). PDCP has an active and
innovative management group, headed by its President, a cofounder of PDCP
associated with PDCP-s management since its inception who is an outstanding
development banker of international repute. He is ably supported by a team
of competent executives.
10.       PDCP-s staff resources represent a blend of people with different
backgrounds and academic disciplines enabling PDCP to effectively meet the
varied needs of its clients. As of March 1980, professionals comprised 68% of
its total manpower complement of 267. In the late 1970s, PDCP faced a high
turnover rate but through various measures taken, it has been able to reduce
its turnover rate from 23.5% in 1976 to 11.9% in 1979. Given the rapidly
/1 Eight Directors represent Filipino stockholders and the remaining three
foreign stockholders. Besides stockholders respresentatives Filipino
Directors include one representative each from the Government, the
guarantor of the IBRD and ADB loans, to PDCP, and the PNB, the conduit
for such loans.



ANNEX 4
- 72 -                              Page 4
growing demand for the industrial financing skills possessed by PDCPs' staff,
staff turnover rate is likely to remain relatively high.  Ilowever, on account
of the regular staff recruitment and training programs in effect, PDCP's
standards are not likely to be adversely affected by staff turnover.  PDCP's
training institute is well organized, and its programs are pragmatic,
professional and issue-oriented.
11.       Policies, Procedures and Standards.  PDCP-s Statement of General
Business polices adopted by its Board of Directors in 1964, inter alia pro-
vides for the usual financial prudence limits, portfolio diversification,
sound financial and dividend policies and investment criteria (Annex 4,
Attachment 1). These provisions are further supplemented by Board resolu-
tions. The Policy Statement sets out a set of investment and project selec-
tion criteria and provides that investments should be consistent with Govern-
ment priorities and sound financial practices. The economic and developmental
impact of a project are among important criteria used by PDCP in determining a
project's merit. PDCP has consistently followed the policy guidelines both in
letter and in spirit.
12.       PDCP's project appraisals are comprehensive, and satisfy the
criteria for appraisal set out in its policy statement. PDCP fully examines
the technical, financial, and economic viability of the projects it assists,
calculates financial and economic rates of return of the projects, and
examines other economic indicators of project viability on a consistent and
invariable basis.  PDCP's technical appraisal is generally comprehensive, but
so far it has not been very successful in encouraging the project proponents
to modify their project designs so as to adopt technology better suited to
local conditions, particularly labor intensive technology. This is largely
due to the fact that PDCP in the past concentrated on BOI approved projects
which, to modify, would have required fresh BOI approval. With improvements
in BOI's own economic analysis capabilities resulting from assistance being
rendered under SAL I, PDCP should be able, in the future, to influence project
design more materially. At negotiations an understanding was reached with the
Central Bank that it would require PDCP to endeavor to play a more meaningful
role in project design and choice of technology by getting involved with
project at its formulation stage. PDCP's internal project processing pro-
cedures are streamlined which ensure careful and quick consideration of all
relevant aspects of each project, including a thorough scrutiny of each
appraisal by the senior management.
13.       PDCP's'supervision policies and procedures are basically sound.
Supervision of projects commences from the time of signing of the loan/
investment agreement and ends when the loan is fully repaid or the invest-
ment is sold. PDCP staff are required to visit each project at least once
every quarter during construction and at least once a year when the project
is in operation; clients are required to submit progress and financial
reports on a regular periodic basis. Recently, however, because of the
rapid expansion in PDCP's portiolio, iull regular supervision has often been



ANNEX 4
- 73 -                           Page 5
restricted to projects experiencing difficulties.  The disadvantage of this
practice is that problems are not spotted until they have seriously affected
project performance. PDCP fully recognizes this shortcoming and has already
initiated organizational changes and is assigning a larger number of staff for
supervision work. In addition, PDCP has initiated an action program to deal
with and assist problem accounts. The results of these problem-solving
efforts have so far been encouraging (para. 21).
Operations
14.       PDCP's project assistance can be classified into five major cate-
gories: loans, equity investments, guarantees, loan syndications and under-
writing. In addition, PDCP actively operates on the short-term money
market. The table below summarizes financial assistance approved by PDCP

since 1975, details are given in Annex 4, T-2. From an initial level of
P 40.7 million in 1964, PDCP's annual financial assistance has steadily
increased to P 710.8 million by the end of 1979. Loans are by far the most
important form of assistance and accounted for over 55% of the assistance
approved since 1975 by amount and about 80% by number of projects. Loan
syndications contribute 22% of the amount of assistance but, by number, such
transactions are not very significant (6%).
15.       Loan Operations.  Since its inception, PDCP has approved 715
projects and provided P 1,843 million ($246 million equivalent) in term
loans (Annex 4, T - 3). These are all medium- to long-term (5-12 years) loans
in local and foreign currencies. Following an uncertain fluctuating pattern
between 1974 and 1977, PDCP's loan approvals recorded a dramatic increase in
1978, and have since maintained a rising trend. For the period 1975-77, loan
approvals were adversely affected by the general worldwide economic recession
and increased competition for prime clients from the newly established OBUs.
However, adoption of an aggressive business promotion strategy, realignment of
its lending terms to market rates and streamlining of internal procedures
enabled PDCP to more than recapture its share of business.
16.       PDCP's lending operations are divided into three categories: (a)
general loans which comprise loans to medium- and large-scale industries; (b)
small business loans; and (c) agri-business loans. Although small loans have
rapidly grown since 1975, general loans still constitute the bulk (over 85%)
of PDCP's loans. The general loans are largely (over 85%) in foreign
currency, while the small business loans are invariably in pesos. Of total
loan approvals in 1979, about 66% went to the manufacturing sector; the rest
were for electricity, transportation, communications, construction, agricul-
ture and mining. There has been a relatively stable sectoral distribution of
project assistance. The pattern of geographical distribution of PDCP-s loans
is satisfactory; more than 76% of the loans made since 1975 are located in
areas outside Metro Manila. Characteristics of PDCP's loan approvals are
shown in Annex 4, T - 4.
17.       Other Forms of Assistance.  PDCP sometimes makes equity investments
in conjunction with its loans. New equity investment approvals since 1975
represent only 2.4% of total assistance approved. PDCP also guarantees the
repayment of foreign and local currency obligations incurred by manufacturing
companies for import of machinery and equipment. Guarantee approvals in 1979
amounted to a record high P 68.7 million. Since 1975, PDCP has managed 33
syndications of long-term loans involving US$34.7 million in foreign exchange
and and P 148.2 million in domestic currency. Finally, to help develop the
local capital market, PDCP has engaged itself in underwriting the sale or
private placement of corporate debt or equity securities. Such commitments



ANNEX 4
page 7
- 75 -
have been made both on a "firm" and a "best efforts" basis. The volume of
such operations has expanded over the years, from a total of P 5 million for
two projects in 1975 to P 107 million for 10 projects in 1979.
Financial Aspects
18.       Financial Position.  PDCP's balance sheets as of December 31, 1979
and March 31, 1980 are summarized in Annex 4, T - 5. After experiencing slow
growth in 1976 and 1977, PDCP's total assets recorded much faster annual
growth rates of 10.6% for 1978, 22% for 1979, and 15% for the first quarter of
1980 (annualized). The composition of PDCP-s assets has experienced little
significant change over the past few years. The outstanding term loan
portfolio has consistently accounted for 70-80% of assets over 1975-80 period.
The trend of PDCP's liability composition over the years has been parallel to
that of its assets and therefore has remained relatively unchanged. Long-term
debts comprise the major proportion (60-75%), while current liabilities
comprise 10-20% of total liabilities and equity. Except for 1976, when PDCP
last increased its share capital by P 11.9 million, the almost 50% build-up
in PDCP's equity between 1975 and 1980 (March 31) has been through plowback of
income. Long-term/debt equity ratio as of March 31, 1980 stood at 5.6:1, the
highest since 1975, but is well within the contractual limit of 8:1 set by the
Bank. Similarly, PDCP's short-term debt/equity ratio has been within the
contractual limit of 2:1 with an actual ratio of 1.5:1 as of March 31, 1980.
19.       PDCP's liquidity position has been under pressure since 1976;
between 1976 and 1980 (March 31) the debt service cover declined from 1.2 to
1.0 and the liquidity ratio declined from 1.5 to 1.2 (Annex 4, T - 7). The
tight liquidity position is the result of: declining loan collections (see
para. 21); PDCP's policy of meeting the corresponding maturing obligations to
its borrowers for projects in arrears pending final agreement with the
subloans on restructuring arrangements; and a general shortage of long-term
peso funds. PDCP is taking a number of steps to improve its liquidity
including raising larger amounts of medium- and long-term peso funds in the
form of notes and bonds (this effort should be facilitated by financial sector
policy reforms of 1980), and improving loan collections. The liquidity aspect
would however be closely watched by ADFU during subsequent monitoring of PDCP.
20.       Operating Performance.  PDCP-s summarized income statements for
1975-80 (March 31) are given in Annex 4, T - 6. PDCP's major source of income
has remained interest income from loans which accounts for about 80-85% of the
the total gross income; the remaining 15-20% comes from short-term operations,
capital market operations and other sources. PDCP's net income increased from
P 16.5 million in 1975 to P 24.5 million in 1979. As a percentage of
average total assets, net income has increased from 2.0% in 1975 to 2.4% in



ANNEX 4
-76-                                 Page 8
1978, with a slight decline to 2.2% in 1979.  Net income as a percentage of
average equity has remained at around 16.0% for the period 1975-79. Net
income is expected to grow at a faster rate in the next few years when the
full impact of the growth in PDCP's lending operations in 1978 and 1979 is
reflected.
21.       Quality of Portfolio.  The status of PDCP's loan portfolio is
presented in Annex 4, T-8. As of July 31, 1980, total loan portfolio stood at
P 1,009.7 million (US$134.6 million) to 368 clients. In the past five
years, the quality of PDCP's loan portfolio has shown some signs of deter-
ioration, with total arrears rising from 4.3% of total portfolio in 1975 to
7.2% in 1978; the ratio of portfolio affected by these arrears has risen from
14.9% in 1976 to 18.7% in 1978. Besides the overall unsatisfactory macro
performance of the Philippine industrial sector, the factors that have contri-
buted to the rise in loan arrears include a business recession in 1975 and
1976, the liquidity crunch in the banking sector in 1978 and 1979 and the
declining intensity in PDCP-s supervision of projects. In 1979, PDCP-s mana-
gement formulated a comprehensive program to reduce past dues. These
remedial actions consisted of improving internal procedures, increasing staff
strength and competence in PDCP's supervision group, detailed review of past
due accounts with a view to assessing and providing the necessary package of
assistance needed by clients experiencing problems capable of being remedied,
and identifying projects for foreclosure proceedings. These remedial actions
were discussed in detail by PDCP management with both the Bank and ADFU staff
prior to and during their implementation. These actions have produced
encouraging results as indicated by reduction in loan arrears to 6b7% in 1979
and 6.4% in 1980 (October). However, part of this reduction was the result
not of cash collections, but finalization of long overdue restructuring of a
number of accounts. Real improvement in the quality of PDCP's portfolio would
lie in larger future loan collections. ADFU will monitor closely PDCP's
collection performance and suggest, if necessary, appropriate additional
measures. PDCP has consistently provided for possible losses on loans and
equity investments. Total provisions made for this purpose amounted to
P 22.9 million or 2.4% of the portfolio as of Mtarch 31, 1980 which is
adequate. There have been no defaults on PDCP-s guarantees.
Prospects
22.       Development Strategy for 1980s.  PDCP-s development strategy for
the 1980s will be to continue operating as a development finance company,
with an operating and financing strategy consistent with national develop-
ment goals. PDCP plans to (a) step up its contribution to the domestic
capital formation by establishing higher targets of financial assistance to
productive enterprises; (b) diversify its own sources of foreign exchange
funds in line with PDCP's increased efforts to service its clients growth
and the more complex capital requirements that such growth would entail; (c)
continue to diversify the regional distribution of its portfolio; (d) fur-
ther expand its Small- and Medium-Business Term Lending (S4BTL) through con-
tinued accreditation with IGLF; and (e) continue to train its own staff and



ANNEX 4
Page 9
to disseminate PDCP's expertise to both the private and the public sectors
through training programs tailored to their needs.  PDCP has been able to
achieve its earlier development strategy, initiated in 1977, with a
significant degree of success, and assisted by the financial and industrial
sector reforms of 1980, it should be in a better position to refine and
diversify its project selection process and achieve an even greater degree of
economic impact in its financing.
24.       Business Expectations and Forecasts.  Although PDCP's peso lending
for small industries is expected to increase, its major thrust in operations
is expected to continue to be lending in foreign currency for medium- to
large-scale industries. Based on official statistics, PDCP has projected the
market for its lending operations to grow at a compounded annual rate of 22%,
or from US$459 million in 1982 to US$1,025 million in 1984 for foreign
currency loans, and from P 1,242 million in 1980 to P 2,777.8 million in
1984 for its peso loans. PDCP's own share in the potential market is expected
to improve from 31% in 1980 to 38% in 1984. Foreign currency loan approvals
are expected to reach P 381.9 million in 1980 and further grow to P 504.2
million in 1982 and P 664.4 million in 1984. Peso loans are projected to
rise from P 125.0 million in 1980 to P 189.2 million in 1982 and P 225.4
million in 1984. Including approvals of other types of assistance (equity
investments, underwriting/private placements, guarantees and loan syndi-
cations), the total magnitude of PDCP's assistance is expected to reach
P 1.0 billion in 1980 (P 740 million in 1979), P 1.4 billion in 1982 and
P 2.2 billion in 1984. Details of PDCP's projected operations are given in
Annex 4, T-9.
24.       Resource Requirement   Over the period, 1980 to mid-1983, PDCP would
require net additional foreign exchange resources of US$180 million to meet
its targets for foreign currency loans. PDCP has applied for a $50 million
allocation under the proposed Bank loan and it proposes to raise the remaining
$130 million from other sources such as IFC, ADB and CDC. Considering that
PDCP has raised funds from these sources in the past, the target of mobilizing
additional foreign currency resources appears capable of being reached. PDCP
is already discussing with IFC the possibility of raising $30 million. It
expects to finance its new peso loans largely through IGLF and from internal
cash generations. If, however, the long-term peso market develops, which is
expected as a result of the financial reforms, PDCP would endeavor to raise
long-term peso resources from the market and expand its peso lending.
25.       Projected Financial Condition.  Based on its business forecasts,
PDCP has projected its total assets to grow from P 1,229 million in 1979 to
P 3,830 million in 1984, mostly through an increase in its loan portfolio
(Annex 4, T-10). The long-term loan portfolio is projected to continue to
constitute the bulk of the assets (72-77%). Likewise the bulk of PDCP's
long-term liabilities are projected to be foreign currency borrowings. The
forecasted stockholders equity will maintain its 10-11% share in total



-78-                                   ANNEX 4
Page 10
resources. Beginning 1981, PDCP would need additional annual cash contri-
butions (P 25-30 million per annum) to enable it to remain within the
contractual long-term debt/equity ratio (Annex 4, T-13). Before approving
accreditation, ADFU would require PDCP to submit its plans for future
increases in its capital and compliance with the contractual long-term debt/
equity ratio.
26.       PDCP's net earnings are projected to increase from P 24.5 million
in 1979 to P 65.5 million in 1984, with an average annual increase of P
8.2 million (Annex 4, T-ll). Notable, however, is the sharp growth of
earnings from P 33.5 million for 1981 to P 47.0 million for 1982 due to
expansion in operations. Projected loan operations are forecasted to continue
to generate the biggest share in earnings and projected net income will range
between 1.8-2.1% of average assets and 16.7-19.1% of net worth.

January 27, 1981

PHILIPPINES
INDUSTRIAL FINANCE PROJECT
Private Development Corporation of the Philippine
Statement of General Business Policies /1
1.        Economic Role of PDCP - The Corporation shall assist in the
economic development of the Philippines. To this end it will assist in the
development of private productive enterprises in the country by providing
medium, long-term and equity financing to such enterprises, assisting in the
development of a wider market for corporate securities, encouranging a wider
distribution of ownership in such enterprises.
2.        Privately-Controlled Industrial and Other Productive Enterprises -
The term "privately-controlled" as used in the Articles of Incorporation
will be taken to mean all enterprises wqhich are privately operated and
managed, and where private ownership is a controlling interest (at least 2/3
of the voting stock). The mere fact that such firms have secured loans from
government financial institutions, or that the government holds a minority
equity position (up to 1/3 of the voting stock) will not exclude such firms
from receiving assistance from the Corporation.
3.        Non-Control of Enterprises Financed - The Corporation will not
seek to control any of the enterprises that it finances and will not
participate in the management of such firms.
4.        Diversification of Investments - In managing its portfolio, the
Corporation will endeavour to diversify its investments - in terms of the
type of industries being financed, the location of such industries, and as
to whether funds are used for medium or long-term loans, or for equity
investments. Having regard to its developmental objectives, the Corporation
will give priority to increasing the number of enterprises it finances, to
assisting medium and small firms as well as large, and to diversifying the
ownership of client-enterprises. In this connection, the Corporation will
seek to broaden the enterpreneurial base in the country, by making special
efforts to assist qualified enterprises that, by virtue of their size, age
or ownership, do not have as ready access to other financial institutions as
the larger, better established industrial enterprises in the country.
/1 Adopted by the Board on March 10, 1964 and amended by Board Resolutions
No. 264-66 on July 15, 1966; No. 333-68 on January 16, 1968; No. 456-70
on June 19, 1970; and No. 559-72 on September 12, 1972.



-80 -                            ANNEX 4
Attachment 1
Page 2
5.   Maximum Investment
(a)  The Corporation shall not normally make an investment in any one
investment enterprise, if at the time or as a result thereof the
total investment (as hereunder defined) of the Corporation in
such investment enterprises shall exceed an amount equivalent to
25% of the sum paid-in unimpaired capital, surplus and reserves to
the Corporation and the amount of the AID loan not due and payable.
(b)  In general, the Corporation's investment in any one investment
enterprise shall not exceed 50% of the total assets of the
investment enterprise including PDCP-s investment.
(c)  For the purposes of this policy, the term "investment" shall refer
to loans, equity participations and guarantees.
6.        Retirement of Equity Investments - The Corporation looks upon its
equity investments more as an assistance to productive enterprises and will
seek to retire and dispose of its shares, as soon as this becomes feasible
and a fair price can be obtained for its shares.
7.        Diversification of Ownership - The ownership of the Corporation
will be diversified as widely as possible, and reasonable efforts will be
taken to prevent any one person, entity, or group from dominating the owner-
ship or management of the Corporation.
8.        Reserve and Dividend Policy - The Corporation will pursue a
prudent dividend policy that will enable it to provide shareholders with a
fair return on their investments while at the same time ensuring that its
surplus reserves grow at a reasonable rate. The Corporaton will from time
to time appropriate a portion of its retained earnings to surplus reserves.
The Corporation will also provide by charges against income valuation
reserves consistent with the size and quality of the Corporation's loan and
equity portfolio.
9.        Exchange Risk - The Corporation will protect itself adequately
against foreign exchange risks arising out of its operations.
10.       Criteria in Processing Loan Applications - Loan applications filed
with the Corporation will be processed on the basis of their economic and
business merits. The projects will be analyzed as to their economic
desirablity, technical feasibility, financial soundness, management
competence, etc., to ensure that only sound productive expenditures are
financed.
11.       Technical Assistance to New Enterprises - The Corporation will
endeavor to provide the necessary technical service to the enterprises being
financed whenever necessary.
AEPID
11/3/80

PHILIPPINES
INDUSTRIAL FINANCE PROJECT
Philippine investments Systems Organization (PIS0)
A Review of Institutional, Operational and Financial Aspects
Background, Objectives and Functions
1.        PISO, a privately-owned investment house, was set up in 1974.  Its
charter, embodied in its Articles, authorizes it to undertake all the func-
tions of a development bank/investment house including project promotion,
development financing and all other usual functions of an investment house
including underwriting and merchandising of securities, syndications, etc.
Like most other investment houses in the country, PISO has not actively
pursued a typical investment banking role. During the initial years of its
establishment, it concentrated on short-term money market operations and some
loan syndications.  Starting in 1978 it diverted a substantial portion of its
business to term lending. This change was facilitated by a $15 million loan
from the Bank /1. Consequently, PISO is now one of the three institutions in
the country specializing in medium/long-term financing of industry. PISO, as
a matter of policy, assists all types and sizes of viable industrial projects
in their establishment, expansion or rationalization.
PISO's Role in the Economy
2.        Being a relatively young institution, PISO-s actual quantitative
contribution to the country s economic development is relatively small. Since
most of the projects assisted by PISO are not yet in operation, the best
indication of PISO's economic impact would be the contributions these projects
are expected to make when completed. The 93 projects assisted by PISO up to
March 31, 1980 involve a total investment of P 1.9 billion with an average
investment of P 20 million per project of which P 9.4 million (47%) is
provided by PISO. These projects are estimated to create over 27,000 new jobs
with an average capital investment of about P 70,000 ($9,335) per job.
These projects are also likely to have a beneficial impact on the balance of
payments position of the country and are estimated to earn/save foreign
exchange to the tune of P 485 million through net exports and/or import
substitution.
/_ Loan No. 1555-PH.



ANNEX 5
95                               Page 2
3.        Resource Allocation.  PIS0-s financing is provided after a detailed
scrutiny of the viability of projects and is instrumental in promoting sound
industrial development. The estimated financial and economic rates of return
based on a representative sample of projects assisted by PISO are very
satisfactory as shown below:
FRR        ERR
(No. of projects)
Less than 15%          1           -
15 to 24%              9           4
25% to 50%            16           9
50% and over           3          13
Total              29         26
Projects assisted by PIS0 are well distributed by subsectors of industry.
Balanced regional dispersal of industrial investment is also a major develop-
ment objective pursued by PIS0 but so far it has achieved only limited success
in this endeavor and over 50% of its assistance has gone to projects located
in the Metro Manila area. This in part reflects the general pattern of
industrial investment in the country which is heavily biased in favor of Metro
Manila, and a lack of a branch network. To improve the geographical distribu-
tion of its assistance, PIS0 is entering into technical and financial tie-ups
with selected private development banks located outside of Metro Manila area.
4.        Assistance to Small Business.  The bulk of PISO's financial
assistance goes to medium- and large-scale enterprises. However, cognizant of
the economic benefits of promoting small-scale industries, PIS0 has started a
small business assistance program. PISO has so far made 34 SMI loans for
P 27.5 million. This is over 52% of the total number of loans made, but
only 11.2% of the amount. Besides providing financial assistance, PISO
actively assists and advises small entrepreneurs in the course of formulation,
implemention and operation of their projects. PISO has also set-up an
affiliate company /1 (PISO Leasing Corporation), to provide leasing assistance
mainly to SMI. Through its proposed tie-ups with private development banks,
PIS0 would further expand its financial and technical assistance to small
enterprises.
/1 PISO Leasing Corporation was set-up in 1979 in collaboration with the
International Finance Corporation which has invested $152,000 in equity
and provided a loan of $5.0 million.



AiNEX 5
- 96 -                               Page 3
5.        Resource Mobilization.  As of March 31, 1980 total resources mobi-
lized by PISO stood at P 387.4 million (Annex 5, T-1). The bulk of these
resources (over 65%) came from official sources such as the Bank and ADB ($15
million or P 112.5 million each), US Eximbank ($1.5 million or P 11.2
million), and IGLF (P 11 million). PISO0s own resource mobilization efforts
are represented by its net worth (P 45.0 million) and short-term money
market borrowings (P 83.9 million) aggregating a modest figure of P 128.9
million. Like most other financial institutions in the Philippines, PISO's
ability to mobilize resources has been severely constrained by the highly
restrictive and regulated monetary and interest rate policies of the Govern-
ment and the Central Bank. With the changes in financial sector policies
introduced in 1980, the environment would be more conducive to long-term
resource raising in the future. PISO has, however, been quite successful in
loan syndications and has so far managed 24 syndications for a total of P
647.5 million, representing more than twice its own total long-term resources.
Institutional Aspects
6.        Ownership, Management and Staff.  PISO's existing paid-in capital of
P 33.8 million /1, is subscribed 30% by its only foreign stockholder,
Private Investment Company for Asia, S.A. (PICA), an internationally owned
private institution and 70% by 16 local stockholders. The Land Bank of the
Philippines (LBP) is among the largest local stockholders with 14.5% holding
while six other diverse groups together own about 53% of the capital. PISO is
commited to further broaden the base of its stockholding and has reached an
understanding with ADFU that it will raise its paid-in capital by a further
P 10 million by the end of 1981 in a mnanner that the maximum ownership of
any single Filipino stockholder group, except LBP, would not exceed 12%. In
this context, efforts would be made to invite participation of trust/pension
funds.
7.        PISO is a professionally and competently managed institution.  Broad
policy guidelines are set by its Board of Directors, comprising 11 members
including 3 representing its foreign stockholder (PICA), 7 representing the
Filipino stockholders /2 and one nominee of PNB./3 The Board consists of
directors of diversified background and has proved to be an effective and a
cohesive body providing valuable guidance to PISO's management. The
management team is headed of PISOs Chairman-President who is an experienced
/1 PISO's paid-in capital was increased in 1978 by P 10 million, which
was a condition of the Bank loan. PISO net worth as of March 31, 1980
stood at P 45.0 million.
/2 One of them is a Deputy Minister of Finance.
/3  PNB is the conduit for the Bank and ADB loans as the Government could
not guarantee borrowings of a private institution like PISO.



ANNEX 5
_ 97 -                            Page 4
development banker and who has provided effective management leadership.
Between March 1978 and March 1980 PISO increased its total staff from 55 to
72, including an increase in professional staff from 38 to 44.  The staff
possess varied disciplines, including engineering, finance, economics, law
and banking. PTSO has agreed with ADFU to recruit additional staff as
needed by its expanding operations and hire a manpower resource development
specialist to assist it in meeting its staff needs and organize staff
training on a systematic basis.
8.        Policies, Procedures and Standards.  PISO's Board of Directors
adopted a Policy Statement in March 1979, the draft of which was earlier
discussed and agreed upon with the Bank (Annex 5, Attachment 1); no amendments
have since been made. PISO has so far generally complied with the provisions
of its Policy Statement. There were, however, two transactions where PISO's
Board decided, as exceptions, to exceed the single enterprise exposure limit
(25% of PISO0s net worth) in view of the back-to-back bank guarantees that
were available for the amount in excess of the limit. ADFU and PISO have
reached an understanding that in future such exceptions would not be made;
and this was confirmed at negotiations.
9.        PISO's appraisal procedures are well developed and the normal
processing time ranges between six to eight weeks. The quality of PISO's
appraisals has improved in the past two years both in respect of the depth of
coverage and quality of judgement. PISOs appraisal reports generally include
an objective assessment of the relevant aspects of the project's viability.
The economic viability of the project is usually tested in terms of its
expected contributions to the economy in the form of value added, exports, net
foreign exchange savings and earnings, employment generation and regional
development. Economic and financial rates of return are calculated for all
loans above $750,000. PISO's engineering evaluation of projects gives due
consideration to the suitability of the technology proposed, economic scale of
operations and the project's environmental impact.
10.       PISOTs organization, policies and procedures for project supervision
are not yet fully developed. ADFU has observed that although the department
responsible for supervision does undertake project visits and obtains periodic
progress and financial reports, this is not done in an organized and regular
basis. ADFU has, therefore, suggested and PISO has agreed to: (i) strengthen
the supervision organization and prepare and adopt a comprehensive project
supervision plan; (ii) appoint qualified staff in adequate number for project
supervision work; and (iii) prepare a Project Supervision Manual for the
guidance of its staff, spelling out the supervision policy and procedures.
Progress made in this regard reviewed at loan negotiations is satisfactory.



ANNEX 5
-98 -                              Page 5
Operations
11.       Trend of Operations.  PISO provides financial assistance in various
forms but medium- and long-term loans and loan syndications have so far
accounted for the bulk (90%) of PISO's long-term operations; direct equity
investments, guarantees, underwriting and private placements have accounted
for the remaining 10%. In addition, PISO is very active on the short-term
money market. As shown in Annex 5, T-2 and summarized below, after 1977 the
importance of loan syndications in relation to total operations has gradually
declined, which reflects availability of loanable funds from the Bank and ADB.
Although this temporarily diminished its role as a catalyst for funds, PISO
continues to assign a high priority to loan syndications in its total
operations.  In fact, beginning 1981, it projects a substantial growth in
syndications (para. 16).
