Total    25  100   392.3 100.0  86  100.0  602.2 100.0 111 100.0  994.5 100.0
12.       Features of Loan Operations.  Characteristics of PISO's industrial
loans are shown in Annex 5, T-3.  Since the start of its operations PISO has
approved 65 loans, for an aggregate of P 244.5 million, falling into two
distinct categories: (a) regular loans; and (b) small business loans. The
regular loans constitute the core of PISO-s lending operations and account for
47.7% of total number and 88.8% of total amount of loan approvals. These
loans were made to relatively large industries and were overwhelmingly in
foreign exchange. The small business loans, in contrast, were made to
small- and medium-scale industries almost entirely financed under IGLF. The



ANNEX 5
- 99 -                           Page 6
average loan size was P 7.0 million for regular loans and P 0.8 million
for small loans. About 56% of the loans made were for subprojects in the
manufacturing sector followed by telecommunications (19.9%), transport
(10.4%), mining (9.2%) and the service sectors (4.4%). PIS0 is not over
exposed in any one sector or subsector and its portfolio is well diversified.
PISO-s operations so far indicate a strong preference for well established
clients with about 90% of loans going to existing enterprises. This is not
quite in line with the objectives enunciated in PISO-s Policy Statement (para.
8). PIS0-s management attributes this bias to the necessity of developing a
sound portfolio prior to entering into riskier investments and has expressed
its intention of allocating a much larger proportion of its loans to new
projects/entrepreneurs in the future. ADFU would reach a firm standing on
this matter with PISO prior to accreditation.
Financial Aspects
13.       Financial Position.  A significant feature of PIS0-s financial
position is the rapid growth in its assets/resources (Annex 5, T-4). Between
December 31, 1977 and 1979 total assets jumped from P 125.5 million to
P 231.3 million for an annual growth rate of about 35%. Most of the
additional investment was in the long-term portfolio which rose from a meagre
P 5.3 million in 1977 to P 85.0 million in 1979. Consequently, the
long-term assets as a percentage of total assets increased from 5.2% in 1977
to 38.6% in 1979. This trend was sustained in the first quarter of 1980. The
growth in the portfolio was almost entirely financed through term borrowings
and as a consequence PISO0s long-term debts grew from P 4.2 million in 1977
to P 80.8 million in 1979 and P 87.4 million in March 1980. At the same
time, current liabilities and net worth grew modestly. In terms of its
agreement with the Bank, PISO is required not to exceed a total debt /1 to
equity ratio of 10:1 and long-term debt /1 to equity ratio of 4:1. In
calculating its contractual debt/equity ratios, PISO has been excluding from
its total debts that portion which it incurred against a counter commercial
bank/IGLF guarantee. However, ADFU has reached an understanding with PISO
that for calculation of its contractual debt/equity ratios, all debts as
defined in the Loan Agreement with Bank, would be taken into account.
However, PISO has been well within its contractual limits with a total
debt/equity ratio of 4.2:1 and long-term debt/equity ratio of 2.2:1. PISO has
maintained satisfactory levels of liquidity and debt service coverage in the
past three years and has complied with the understandings reached with the
Bank on the subject.
14.       Financial Performance.  Interest income on medium- and long-term
loans which contributed only 7.2% to PISO-s total gross income in 1977
accounted for 30.6% of the gross income in 1979 and rose even higher to
45.9% at the end of the first quarter of 1980.  This was directly attributable
/1 Including guarantees.



ANNEX 5
- 100-                                 Page 7
to growth in its long-term portfolio. Although PISO's interest costs and
administrative expenses have been rising, gross income has grown at an even
faster rate.  Consequently, the net income increased from P 3.5 million in
1977 to P 4.6 million in 1978 and P6.1 million in 1979 representing 13.3%,
13.6% and 14.7% of net worth respectively.  As percentage of total assets, net
income ranged between 2.9% and 2.8%. PISO's Board has so far followed a
conservative dividend policy and paid cash dividends at 8.0% each for 1978 and
1979, plowing back over 50% of the income.  The unaudited operating results of
the first quarter of 1980 indicate that the profitability trend of the past
three years would be maintained.  PISO's operating results for 1975-79 and
first quarter of 1980 are summarized in Annex 5, T-5.
15.        Quality of Portfolio.  Even allowing for the rather short history cf
its operations, PISO's portfolio is in a remarkably a good state. As of June
30, 1980, PISO's total outstanding loan portfolio stood at P 105.5 mili4on
to 47 client companies (Annex 5, T-6). So far PISO has not faced any serious
problems in loan collections. As of June 30, 1980 past dues amounted to only
P 0.3 million or 0.3% of the total loan portfolio; only 1.7% of the port-
folio was affected by these defaults. More than 50% of the arrears arose in
small business loans but were due for less than 90 days.  There was only one
default (P 14,600) on the regular loans for which collection arrangements
were being worked out.  Similarly, there were no defaults on PISO-s short-term
money market or guarantee operations. Even though PISO does not expect any
eventual portfolio losses, it has regularly made provisions for losses. As of
December 31, 1979, total provisions for losses stood at P 7.0 million or
3.9% of total short and long-term portfolio.
Prospects
16.        Projected Operations.  Based on actual operations in the first half
of 1980 and the pipeline of applications, PISO expects to approve new loans
amounting to P 196 million in 1980, almost a 100% increase over the 1979
level. Between 1981 and 1984 however, a modest growth of 14-19% is expected
in new approvals. Except for 1980 when peso loans were expected to increase
six fold due to the increased upper limit of loans under the IGLF scheme, the
rate of growth in foreign currency loans is faster than in peso loans.
Approvals of foreign exchange loans are projected at P 196.5 million in
1981, P 237.0 million in 1982, P 273.0 million in 1983 and P 315.0
million in 1984, while peso loans are projected at P 34.0 million, P 37.6
million, P 41.0 million and P 44.0 million respectively. PISO has
projected even faster growth in its other forms of assistance such as
guarantees, underwriting and syndications.  However, no new equity investments
are budgeted.  In order to achieve its ambitious business targets, PISO will
make necessary changes in its organization, staffing and business strategy.
It will promote its business relationship with private development banks to be
able to capture new business particularly outside the Metro MIanila area.
Details of PISOs projected operations, 1980-84, are given in Annex 55 T70



ANNEX 5
- 101 -                              Page 8
17.       Resource Requirement.  PISO would need substantial additional
resources to finance its new loan approvals. Its requirements of net addi-
tional foreign exchange resources up to middle of 1983 amount to about $93
million. PIS0 has recently obtained a second loan of $25 million from ADB.
The balance of the requirement is proposed to be met by borrowings from the
Central Bank (ADFU) under the proposed Bank loan ($40 million) and additional
borrowings from ADB in 1983. PISO expects to finance all its new peso loans
from IGLF, as in the past. It is expected that in the future, the financial
environment would be more conducive to mobilizing long-term resources as a
result of the policy reforms introduced in 1980 and those expected in the near
future. Once the new environment materializes, PIS0 should expand its peso
lending by raising funds from the market. ADFU would follow-up this aspect in
the context of its supervision of PIS0.
18.       Projected Financial Statements.  Based on the business targets
discussed in para. 16, PIS0 expects that its assets would increase from
P 231.3 million in 1979 to P 1,495.5 million in 1984 maintaining an
average growth rate of 45% per annum (Annex 5, T-8). The fastest growth of
62% per annum is shown by long-term portfolio rising from P 84.9 million in
1979 to P 956.2 million in 1984. Consequently, the proportion of long-term
assets in total assets will increase from 38.4% in 1979 to 69.5% in 1984. A
similar trend is shown by long-term debts. PIS0 has planned a capital
increase of P0 million in 1981 but even after this increase it would exceed
its present contractual long-term debt/equity ratio of 4:1. Considering the
excellent quality of its portfolio, its strong financial position, the
strength of its management and organization, and the planned capital increase
in 1981, it has been agreed between the Bank and ADFU that PISO's contractual
long-term debt/equity ratio will be relaxed to 6:1.  Since PISO's financial
projections show a need for a further capital increase in 1982, ADFU would
require PISO to furnish it, prior to accreditation, with specific proposals
for future capital increases. Based on its projected financial ratios PISO
would not need to relax its total debt/equity ratio limit presently set at
10:1. The projected liquidity ratios and debt service cover are all within
satisfactory limits.
19.       PISO expects rapid growth in its gross and net income; gross income
is projected to rise from P 24.0 million in 1979 to P 35.1 million in
1980, f 13.9 million in 1982 and P 174.3 million in 1984 (Annex 5, T-9).
As in the past, an increasing proportion of the gross income would be derived
from interest income from medium- and long-term loans. PISO-s projected net
income is P 8.2 million for 1980 (P 6.2 million in 1979), P 16.7 million
for 1981, P 24.7 million for 1982, P 34.8 million for 1983 and P 44.7
million for 1984. This level of net income would yield a return on equity of
17.6 to 32.4%.
AEPID
January 27, 1981

PHILIPPINES
PHILIPPINE INVESTMENTS SYSTEMS ORGANIZATION
Policy Statement
A. PISO's Role and Objectives
1.        Corporate Character.  PISO is a financial institution which is:
essentially Philippine in origin, sentiment and character; committed to the
active promotion of development-oriented investments and to the provision
of financial assistance and related services to ensure the success of such
investments; systems oriented in its approach and operations; and reliant
upon the strength derived from its organization and the diversity of its
ownership. Its primary role will be to mobilize and allocate financial
resources as efficiently as possible to further the process of economic
development.
2.        Objectives.  As a participant in the process of resource
allocation, PISO will actively seek out and assist the development of
productive ventures and income-generating projects undertaken by privately-
controlled /1 business enterprises in various sectors of economic activity.
PISO will assist only those projects and ventures which, in its judgement,
are or will be financially, economically and technically viable, and are
properly organized and soundly managed. PISO will likewise participate
actively in furthering the development of the capital market in the
Philippines especially by way of long-term domestic and foreign resource
mobilization.
3.        Activities.  PISO will provide a full range of financial services,
and technical and management assistance as provided for in its Articles of
Incorporation.  These will include (but not be restricted to):  (a) the pro-
vision of medium- and long-term financing facilities /2 by way of domestic
/1 As used here, the term applies to enterprises in which private-ownership
represents a majority (greater than 50%) share. In this connection,
PISO explicitly recognizes the role that Government participation in
ownership may need to play in accelerating investment in certain areas.
/2 For financing fixed assets and working capital.



ANNEX 5
- 103 -                          Attachment 1
Page 2
and foreign currency loans, guarantees and equity participation;
(b) syndication, underwriting, private placement activities; (c) the
provision of financial packaging, consultancy and investment portfolio
management services; (d) leasing and hire-purchase facilities; and
(e) management consultancy assistance in association with its financial
services. On the resource mobilization side, PISO will continue in
expanding and diversifying the supply of financial instruments to stimulate
savings and channel them into longer-term maturities.
B. Guidelines for Operations
4.        Project Evaluation and Follow-up.  In providing assistance to any
project or enterprise, PISO will apply high professional standards in
evaluating: managerial aspects; technical aspects; market prospects;
financial viability and debt-service capability; and the economic viability
and desirability of such projects/enterprises. It will closely monitor
the implementation of projects and the eventual operations of the enterprises
to which it provides long-term assistance. Whenever it considers
necessary, PISO will provide constructive remedial advice to those enterprises
it perceives in present or eventual difficulty.
5.        Collateral.  In accordance with normal business practices, PISO
will obtain adequate security for the financial assistance it provides. PISO
will also take steps to have adequate insurance coverage obtained for its
security. Its investment decisions, however, will be guided primarily by
considerations relating to project merits.
6.        Investment Exposure Limits.  To ensure that its resources are
widely spread and prudently diversified, PISO shall adopt the following
guidelines:
(a) Total financial commitment /1 (including amounts of outstanding
and undisbursed commitments) to any single enterprise /2 shall not
/1 This term includes equity investments, loans and guarantees (excluding
short-term for the latter 2 categories). The term excludes exposures
covered by hold-out agreement or any firm off-setting arrangements on
debt extended to PISO.
/2 The term enterprise as used in this Statement shall include the enter-
prise and its subsidiaries whenever PISO has any investments in such
subsidiaries.



ANiNEX5
- 104 -                       Attachment 1
Page 3
normally exceed an amount equivalent to 25% of PISO's net
worth (defined as the sum of its paid-in unimpaired capital,
surpluses and reserves).
(b)  Total investment in any enterprise shall not normally exceed
50% of the assets of that enterprise if it is already
established or 75% if the enterprise is being newly established e
(c)  No outstanding equity investment made by PISO in any one enter-
prise shll normally exceed 49% of the paid-in share capital of
that enterprise and 15% of PISO s own net worth.  This limitation
will not apply to a subsidiary, if and when established by PISO.
(d)  The aggregate sum of PISOs equity investments shall not exceed
PISO's own net worth.
7.        Portfolio Diversification and Divestiture
(a)  PISO will endeavor to diversify its loan, equity and quarantee
investments in a wide variety of sectors and industries. It
will also exert its efforts to encourage investment in under-
developed regions and to actively promote the location of
investment outside the Metropolitan Manila Area.
(b)  In accordance with its views on capital market development and to
maximize the use of its own resources, PISO shall dispose of its
equity investments as early as practicable. In selling its
holdings, PISO shall consider, in addition to its own interests.
the interests of the other original participants in the
investments as well as the interests of the concern whose shares
are being disposed of. PISO will endeavor to diversify, as
widely as possible, ownership in the entities in which it invests.
8.        New Entrepreneur/Enterprise Promotion.  In its operations, PTSO
will stress the promotion of new entrepreneurs and enterprises. Broadening
the entrepreneurial base in the Philippines will be one of PIS0-s operational
objectives. To this end, PISO will exert reasonable efforts to: (a) extenJd
financial and technical assistance to small- and medium-scale enterprises:11
and (b) attempt to develop a client base of enterprises which, by virtue of
their size, age, ownership or location, have not enjoyed ready access to
financial assistance as have larger, more established and better-known
corporations.
/1 As defined by the Department of Industry.



ANNEX 1
- 105 -                           Attachment 1
Page 4
9.        Relations with Client Enterprises.  Recognizing the value and
implications of sound lender-borrower relationships, PISO will adhere to
the following policies in its dealing with client firms:
(a) PISO shall not normally seek to control any of the enterprises
that it finances nor to assume primary responsibility for the
management of such enterprises, except in the event that its
interests are jeopardized, in which case, it may take such action
as may be necessary to protect its interests. A higher
percentage of equity holding than mentioned in 6(c) above may
also result from an underwriting commitment undertaken with the
expectation that the eventual investment would be within the
cited limit. Every attempt will be made to reduce the equity
holding to the maximum limit mentioned in 6(c) above as soon as
practicable, all factors considered.
(b) PISO shall requiest its clients to establish and maintain up-to-
date records and accounts in accordance with generally accepted
accounting practices, and furnish whatever information it may
reasonably request on their operations and accounts. Under its
financial contracts, PISO will establish its right to inspect
the enterprises it finances as well as their operations and
accounts.
(c) PISO shall honor the confidences of its clients. In this
connection: no member of PISOs Board, by virtue of his/her
membership, shall have access to information submitted by
clients to PISO0s management or staff when, in the view of
management, such access would prejudice the interest of the
client.
10.       Dispersal of Ownership.  PISO will strive to broaden its
ownership base as widely as possible. PISO will take all possible steps
to prevent any one person, entity or group from dominating its ownership
or management.
C. Financial Policy Guidelines
11.       Capital Structure, Liquidity and Debt Service.  In the conduct of
its business affairs, PISO shall exercise due financial prudence and shall
ensure that its capital structure remains sound, a reasonable liquidity



ANNEX 5
Attachment 1
Page 5
position is maintained and that its cash inflow is always sufficient to
allow a confortable margin for servicing its debt.
12.       Reserves and Dividends.  PT-SO will adhere to a prudent dividend
policy which will allow it to provide a fair return on shareholders invest-
ments and simultaneously allow for building-up retained earnings and reserves
at a reasonable rate.  At a mlnimum, PISO shall plow back at least 40% of
its average net earnings in any given fiscal year as retained earnings.
PISO will provide, by charges against income, valuation reserves consistent
with the size and quality of its loan and equity portfolio. From time to
time, PISO shall appropriate as necessary a portion of its retained earnings
to surplus reserves to ensure that they adequately reflect the size and risks
of its portfolio and to protect its equity against erosion.
13.       Foreign Exchange Risk.  It is PISO's policy not to expose itself
to foreign exchange risks associated with foreign currency obligations/trans-
actions. Accordingly, PISO shall, to the extent possible, pass such risks
on to its clients or arrange, on behalf of clients, for forward cover on
such risks at no cost to itself, or make any other arrangement which
insulates PISO from bearing any part of such risks.
D. Relationships with Government Authorities
14.       PISO shall, at all times, maintain cooperative working relationships
with various government agencies and authorities with respect to its
investment operations and its activities toward capital market development.
Its operations will be consistent with overall government economic policies.
E. Organization and Staffing Policies
15.       In furtherance of its institutional capabilities and to assist
its clients to the fullest possible extent, PISO shall, on a continuing
basis, build and strengthen its management and staff, and evolve an efficient
and sound organizational structure.  To those ends, it shall adopt equitable
and competitive recruitment, employment and compensation policies. PISO
shall provide for continuous training of its staff, realizing as it does, the
importance of human resources.  PISO shall endeavor to ensure that its staff



ANNEX 5
- 107 -                        Attachment 1
Page 6
are of a high professional calibre and represent an appropriate mix of
professional qualifications, ability and experience to permit the
achievement of its corporate objectives in the most efficient manner.
F. Revision of Policies
16.       This Policy Statement may be amended only by the affirmative vote
of at least two-thirds of all directors.
AEPID
January 27, 1981

INDUSTRIAL FINANCE PROJECT
Development Bank of the Philippines (DBP)
A Review of Institutional, Operational and Financial Aspects
Background and DBP's Role in the Economy
1.        DBP was established in 1958 under Republic Act No. 2081 (RA 2081) as
an autonomous Government-owned development bank with a mandate to supply
credit for the reconstruction and development of the agricultural and indus-
trial sectors. In 1964, it was also given the responsibility of promoting the
establishment of private development banks (PDBs) throughout the Philippines.
DBP stands out in the Philippine financial system as the largest supplier of
long-term credit. At the end of 1979, it had total assets of P 22 billion
(US$2.9 billion) which represented 11% of the total assets of the Philippine
financial system; its long-term loan portfolio of P 12.8 billion accounted
for almost half of total outstanding long-term credit of the financial sector.
Through its lending, equity investments and guarantee operations, DBP was
responsible for financing 7.3% /1 of gross fixed capital formation in 1979.
DBP's financing operations (loans, equity investments and guarantees)
encompass almost all segments of the economy. The bulk of DBP's financing,
however, has gone to the private industrial sector covering both large- and
small-scale industrial enterprises. DBP has also been very active in
providing loans to small farmers and in agricultural financing for food and
commercial crops, grain storage and processing, fisheries, livestock and
poultry. Besides, DBP provides finance for real estate (mainly low-cost
housing, hospitals, schools and hotels), shipping, transport, and income-
generating projects of municipal and provincial governments. In addition, DBP
has been instrumental in promoting and developing a large number of PDBs and
rural banks throughout the Philippines. As a key government financial
institution, DBP has also been directed at times by the Government to carry
out special operations, such as lending programs for victims of natural
calamaties. In short, the sheer volume of its operations, the predominantly
long-term nature of its operations, the diversity of its financing services
and the wide spectrum of sectors it serves make DBP an extremely important
institution in the Philippine economy.
/1  Net of refinancing operations.

Institutional Aspects
2.        DBP's Board, Organization, Management and Staff.  The overall
control and direction of DBP is entrusted to a 9 member Board of Governors.
The five full-time governors including the Chairman, and the Chief Executive
Officer (CEO) and the Vice-Chairman constitute the top management team.
However, the present Board is not at full strength as the vacancy recently
created by the death of a full-time Governor has not been filled. Mr. Rafael
A. Sison succeeded Dr. Placido L. Mapa, Jr./l in June 1979 as Chairman and
CEO of DBP in an acting capacity; prior to this promotion he was a full-time
governor of DBP. Mr. Sison is concurrently a Deputy Minister of Industry; in
that capacity, he is actively involved in the formulation of the country's
industrial policies. DBP has a large organization consisting of 30
departments at the Head Office and 62 branches/subbranches scattered all over
the country. Operating departments are divided into four groups each
reporting to one supervising Governor; a grouping of support departments is
under the direct supervision of the Chairman. In the past two years some
important organizational modifications were introduced to: (a) place all
agriculture related departments under the charge of one supervising governor;
(b) reduce the number of departments reporting directly to the Chairman; and
(c) create new departments for better handling of trust operations, large
problem projects, and manpower development. At the branch level, DBP
introduced sectoral specialization among the staff with a view to improving
their operational efficiency.
3.        At the department level, DBP's management has remained relatively
stable. With relatively low staff turnover /2 and increasing experience of
staff, a gradual deepening and improvement in DBP's middle management layer is
discernible.  As of December 31, 1979, DBP had 4,896 staff /3 (with 50%
located in the branches), up from the 3,501 employed as of August 31, 1977.
The increase did much towards alleviating the staff shortages noted by the
Bank in October 1977, but has necessitated extensive training of new recruits.
Recognizing this need, DBP has intensified its training program through the
introduction of an intensive industrial projects course, with the active
assistance from the Bank Group, and through the establishment of a new
department exclusively for manpower development.
/1 Dr. Mapa Jr. is currently an Executive Director of the IMF.
/2  The turnover rate has declined from 3.6% of average staff in 1976 to
2.5% in 1977, 2% in 1978 and further to 1.6% in 1979.
/3 Of whom 60% are professional.

4.        Organization and Staffing for Industrial Lending.  The funds to be
allocated for DBP under the proposed Bank loan would be handled by Industrial
Projects Department I (IPD I). Since the last Bank appraisal of DBP for
industrial lending, IPD I's organization has undergone two changes: (a) split
of project supervision responsibility into Account Servicing Group
(disbursements and billing plus supervision of projects up to completion
stage) and Project Supervision Group (supervision of completed projects); and
(b) creation of four industry divisions within each operating group (Project
Evaluation, Account Servicing and Project Supervision) to introduce industry
specialization. The industry specialization has enabled the staff of IPD I to
acquire a better appreciation of characteristics and problems of specific
industries and thereby has led to better efficiency. However, the splitting
of the project supervision responsibilities has not had any significant
beneficial effect on the efficiency of the project supervision work. More-
over, the reorganization has created a top heavy departmental organizational
structure,/l and hampered the professional growth of IPD I's staff as a result
of excessive functional specialization (para. 6). These problems are planned
to be alleviated with some modifications in the department's organization.
These modifications would regroup Project Evaluation Group and Supervision
Group to form two integrated operating units on the basis of industry
specialization. Each unit would be headed by an Assistant Executive Officer
(AEO) who will supervise both project appraisal and follow-up work.  The
Account Servicing Group would focus only on bookkeeping service.
5.        Operating Policies.  Within the framework of its Charter, the broad
guidelines governing DBP's industrial financing are covered in a statement
agreed upon with the Bank under Loan No. 998-PH (Annex 6, Attachment 1). The
guidelines established therein are augmented by specific Board resolutions.
During the past two years, some major policy changes were introduced with a
view to: (a) enhancing operational efficiency (e.g., increase in discretionary
authority for managers); (b) setting out guidelines on trust operations; and
(c) expanding and liberalizing DBP's financial assistance to PDBs. Moreover,
DBP is in the process of formulating appropriate policies on its refinancing
(para. 12), and "dacion en pago" operations (para. 13); these policies will
be formally adopted on or before June 30, 1981. In addition, the policy on
interest rates is also being rationalized (para 19). The absence of
appropriate policies on these matters has had a disturbing impact on DBP's
operations and finances.
/1 Four layers exist between the department head and project staff.

6.        Industrial Project Appraisal.  IPD I's appraisal capability has
noticeably improved; its appraisal reports are generally comprehensive and the
analytical tools used are appropriate./I DBP-s market analysis has improved
through the industry specialization of appraisal staff and better analytic
framework introduced by market specialists; its evaluation is now more project
oriented than it was previously. The coverage and depth of technical
appraisals done by DBP-s engineers are by and large adequate; when the nature
of projects and/or the scope of feasibility study is found beyond DBP's
inhouse capability, the services of outside independent consultants are used
(e.g. study of rehabilitation program for the cement industry).  Careful
review of the environmental impact of its projects is now standard practice
and compliance with the Government's antipollution regulations is a standard
condition of its financial assistance. FRR and ERR are now calculated for all
projects with DBP's involvement of over $500,000 irrespective of the source of
financing. Sensitivity tests of projects' rates of return have been intro-
duced but need to be sharpened. Assumptions underlying the project analysis,
however, tend to reflect a rather narrow perspective with which appraisal
staff approach project evaluation. This weakness largely stems from the
excessive functional specialization of IPD I's appraisal staff. Currently,
five specialists (management specialist, market analyst, economist, engineer
and financial analyst) are involved in each project appraisal. This arrange-
ment, which was introduced in 1978 when IPD I decided to cope with all aspects
of project appraisal without any support from other departments, has served
its purpose as reflected in the improved analytic framework incorporated in
the appraisal report. DBP has recognized this problem and is already taking
steps to minimize specialization with greater responsibility being given to
experienced officers having broader understanding of characteristics and
problems of specific industries.
7.        Industrial Project Supervision.  Some progress towards increasing
visits to projects and reviewing project performance during the implementation
stages has been made by IPD I. However, the overall quality of supervision
work has not improved at the pace expected at the time of appraisal of the
last Bank industrial loan to DBP due to two basic problems. First, the
compartmentalization of follow-up work by project status and the ill-defined
division of responsibilities spread supervision staff strength rather thin
with consequent difficulty in meeting work schedules. Secondly, the absence
of appropriate policies on refinancing operations resulted in misdirected
emphasis on noncash collections /2 and encouraged supervision staff to give
/1 In view of its improved appraisal capability, DBP now feels confident
enough to take up the role of a syndicate manager of large industrial
loans.
/2 IPD I's "noncash" collection (conversion of loan arrears into a new
loan and/or equity investment) in 1979 totalled nearly P 1.3 billion.



ANNEX 6
- 122 -Page
higher priority to refinancing/restructuring operations than to the necessary
normal supervision work. Appropriate understandings with Central Bank were
reached on this subject at negotiations and are recorded in the "Program of
Action" (Annex 6, Attachment 2).
Operations
8.        Overview.  DBP's financing operations in 1979 reached P 6.7 bil-
lion in loans, guarantees and equity investments, compared to P 4.1 billion
in 1977 and P 4.7 billion in 1978 (Annex 6, T-1). However, about 51% of
DBP's operations in 1979 was accounted for by refinancing operations (para.
12). DBP's lending operations, net of refinancing, slightly rose from the
level of P 2.7 billion in 1977 and 1978 to P 2.8 billion but with a
discernible decline in industrial lending (para. 9). DBP-s lending continues
to cover virtually every sector of the Philippine economy with the industrial
sector being the major beneficiary (50% of cumulative loan approvals) followed
by agriculture (24%), real estate (19%), provincial governments (3%), banking
industry (3%) and others (1%), (Annex 6, T-2). DBP-s guarantee operations,
predominantly used by the industrial sector, declined by 31% in 1979 to
P 859 million (para. 11). Equity investments, on the other hand, surged up
from P 331 million in 1978 to P 1.35 billion in 1979 as a result of
massive conversion of loan arrears into equity investments (para.10).
9.        Industrial Lending.  DBP's approvals of new industrial loans, net of
refinancing of its old loans, dropped from P 1.8 billion in 1977 to P 1.5
billion in 1978 and further to P 1.4 billion in 1979. These approvals
include loans made to clients for refinancing their debts to other lenders
which have also declined in the past three years. Thus, DBP-s lending for
creating new productive capacity and for keeping existing capacity utilized,
in fact, showed no increase over the past three years. Although DBP's lending
for small and medium industry (loans between P 50,000 and P 2,000,000)
increased both in number and amount in 1979 /1 and the share of large loans
(over P 5 million) slightly declined in 1979 (Annex 6, T-3), the overall
average size of industrial loans has increased from P 657,000 in 1977 to
P 835,000 in 1979. This is due to a steady decline in loans for tricycle
financing (on an average P 30,000 per loan) and for very small cottage
industries for which DBP uses PDBs as channel for funds. During 1977-79,
DBP-s industrial lending covered a wide spectrum of industries; the high
proportion of textiles (23% of lending in 1979), mining (21%) and public
utilities (14%) largely reflected DBP's lending to these industries for
refinancing their debts to other creditors. As a matter of policy, DBP
/1 Up from P 163 million for 473 clients in 1978 to P 220 million for 560
clients in 1979.



ANNEX 6
Page 6
-123 -
encourages investments in labor-intensive projects (both small and medium) and
priority is given to labor-intensive projects in its general lending. During
1977-79, DBP-s lending to relatively labor-intensive industries /1 represented
about 43% of its total lending to such industries since 1947.  DBPTs loans in
1977-79 continued to be directed towards Manila and the Central and Southern
Luzon regions. These two areas received 58% (in amount) of total industrial
lending in 1977-79, but in number 72% of industrial loans went to
less-developed regions.
10.       Equity Investments.  DBP acquires equity holdings through exercise
of conversion options, investments in public enterprises under the Govern-
ment-s equity financing programs, and equity investments in PDBs. DBP's
equity investment operations reached a high level oi P 1.35 billion in 1979
(or 43% of cumulative total since 1947). New investments in 1979, however,
accounted for a mere P 46 million (or 3% of the total) in 11 PDBs, 74 rural
banks and one commercial bank; other equity investments totalling P 1.3
billion represented the conversion of loan arrears on large industrial and
hotel accounts /2 into preferred or common shares. The conversions are
done under DBP's policy of providing fehabilitation assistance to its
borrowers. However, DBP seems to have exercised this policy rather too
liberally. This aspect is further discussed in para. 12.
11.       Guarantee Operations.  DBP provides guarantees to cover: (a) foreign
suppliers credits for the import of capital goods; (b) commodity credit
financing from the US and Canada; (c) peso credits extended by commercial
banks to DBP clients; and (d) performance of Filipino contractors working
abroad. DBP-s guarantee operations rose from P 1 billion in 1977 to
P 1.25 billion in 1978 but declined in 1979 to P 859 million. This fluc-
tuation reflects either the entry or absence of a few very large accounts
which affect the overall level of guarantee operations. The average size of
DBP-s guarantees sharply increased from P 18 million in 1977 to P 50
million in 1978 and further to P 100 million as a result of phase out of
commodity credit financing and the absence of domestic credit guarantee in
1979; the latter factor reflects DBP-s recent policy to offer a loan/guarantee
package rather than guarantee only.
/1 These are food processing, apparel, furniture, leather products, metal,
light machinery and cottage industries.
/2 Including such industries as paper (P 304 million converted), shipping
(P 261 million), cement (P 155 million), hotels (P 132 million),
mining (P 91 million), telecommunications (P 90 million), textile
(P 88 million) and others (P 183 million).

12        Refinancing Operations.  A noticeable feature of DBP's operations
in 1977-79 was the increasing trend of refinancing operations as shown
below:
DBP   SUMMARY OF REFINANCING OPERATIONS
(P million)
1977    1978    1979
(1) Conversion of
(a) Old loans to new loans               38     397   1,735
(b) Arrearages to equity invest-
ments                           226      276   1,304
(2) Loans for refinancing clients'
debts to other lenders                  927      573     393
Total                          1,191   1,246   3,432
The bulk of refinancing operations represent DBP's efforts to rehabilitate
problem projects. So far these efforts have not been very fruitful. Many of
these projects had intractable problems, largely caused by the Government's
past industrial sector policies, over which DBP had no control. In the
absence of a package of workable remedial measures (managerial, technical,
marketing and financial), DBP's refinancing package only meant postponement of
inevitable hard decisions. Besides, since conversions of arrearages into
equity investments are treated as collections, DBP's operating units tend to
resort to refinancing rather liberally with a view to boosting their collec-
tion performance. This practice has had an undesirable impact on the serious-
ness and depth of DBP's supervision activity (para. 7). These shortcomings,
in turn, were the result of absence of a comprehensive policy on refinancing
operations. However, DBP is now formulating an appropriate policy on
refinancing which would, among others, state the conditions necessary for
making a project eligible for refinancing assistance, and require a rigorous
reappraisal of the project and a thorough investigation of various measures
needed to improve project performance.
13.       DBP's efforts to rehabilitate problem projects included an arrange-
ment called "dacion en pago" under which borrowers were allowed to surrender
all their assets to DBP in settlement of its claim and DBP in turn, in most
cases, leases these assets back to the same borrowers on concessionary terms.
Since 1977, DBP has approved this type of arrangement with 14 borrowers for



ANNEX 6
- 125-                              Page 8
its claims totalling P 1.3 billion at an effective imputed interest rate of
9-12% p.a., rental payment spreading over 20-25 years. This practice does not
serve DBP's interest as it: (a) encourages borrowers to default in the hope of
getting better terms; (b) provides an opportunity for basically unviable
projects to seek undesirable continuation of protection; and (c) gives no
flexibility to DBP to renegotiate lease contracts in case DBP finds better
options. DBP is presently reviewing the implications of this practice with a
view to adopting appropriate policies to safeguard its interest and avoid ad
hoc decisions.
14.        Impact of Large Industry Financing.  The sheer volume and the
diversity of DBP's operations make it difficult to compile comprehensive data
which would make possible quantification of DBP's contribution to economic
development. However, some conclusions can be drawn by analyzing the loans
made by IPD I during the past two years.  In 1978-79, IPD I approved 83 large
industrial projects with a total capital expenditure of P 2.7 billion, 58%
of which was DBP-financed. These projects were expected to create 18,300 new
jobs at an average cost per job of $18,700.  Twenty-two of these projects were
export-oriented with estimated total export sales of $184 million. The
ex-ante financial rate of return calculated for 76 projects, weighted by
project cost, was about 29%; the ex-ante economic rate of return for 35
projects, weighted by project cost, was about 44%.
15.       Economic Impact of Large Industrial Subprojects Under the Bank
Loans. DBP has received three IBRD loans totalling $150 million for large
industrial projects since 1974. Through these loans, which triggered a
significant expansion of DBP's industrial lending in the mid-1970's, the Bank
has had an important influence on DBP's industrial lending particularly on the
manner in which DBP has dealt with its large industrial projects. Under these
loans, 70 productive industrial projects were financed. The textile industry
accounted for 36% ($52.2 million) /1 of total Bank loans; the remaining loans
were spread among a wide range of industries.  Bank-financed projects are
expected to generate 14,824 jobs /2 at an average capital cost per job of
$29,000. The cost per job varies from $155,000 for soybean processing project
to $1,500-2,000 for garment manufacture. The higher cost/job for Bank-
financed projects compared to that for IPD I's overall operations is due to
the fact that the Bank loans have been used for financing imported capital
goods, which, in turn, are used in projects that are high technology-oriented
/1 The relatively large shares of textile industry mainly reflect a
considerable capacity expansion of the textile industry taken place in the
Philippines in 1975 and 1976. The textile projects covered almost all
segments of the textile industry including nylon filament yarn,
polyester yarn, and both woven and knitted fabrics.
/2 Plus another 7,100 seasonal jobs in a tobacco redrying project and a
cassava processing project.

-126 -                              Page 9
and more capital-intensive than those using domestic equipment.  Forty-three
of the 70 projects planned export sales of at least part of their production;
their aggregate foreign exchange earnings would be about $340 million
annually. In terms of regional distribution, 26 projects are based in
Metro-Manila, 19 in Central and Southern Luzon, 11 in Mindanao and 11 in
Visayas, Bicol and Ilocos with the remaining three projects being for overseas
shipping. The Bank-financed projects are expected to yield acceptable
economic rates of return with an average of 30% weighted by project cost.
Financial Aspects
16.       Financial Position.  Since December 31, 1977 DBP-s total assets have
grown at an average annual rate of 18.6% and reached P 23 billion as of
March 31, 1980 with little change in the asset structure (Annex 6, T-4).
DBP's capital structure remained stable with total debt (including guarantee)
to equity ratio of 5.9:1 as of March 31, 1980, well within the limit of 10:1
agreed with the Bank under the previous loan agreements. However, with the
chronically low loan recoveries DBP's liquidity remains largely dependent on
Government's deposits, most of which are contractually short-term. These
deposits (P 4.4 billion) exceeded DBP's current assets (P 3.9 billion) as
of March 31, 1980. However, under Loan No. 1572-PH, the Government agreed to
give due consideration to DBP-s liquidity position when withdrawals of its
funds are made. Also, to help DBP bolster its cash position and to correct
the imbalance between the maturity structure of its assets and liabilities,
the Government under Loan No. 1572-PH agreed to: (a) raise DBP's paid-in
capital from P 2.5 billion in 1978 to P 4 billion by 1981; (b) help DBP
raise additional long-term resources; (c) assist DBP to liquidate its
investments in three large companies; and (d) arrange for payment of arrears
on loans which were commercially not viable but were made at the Government's
behest. These agreements have been partly implemented, but with mixed results
as discussed in the following paragraphs.
17.       The Government has made no heavy withdrawals on its deposits
maintained with DBP. In fact, the deposits increased from P 3.0 billion in
1977 to P 4.4 billion in 1980. Therefore these deposits can be considered
long-term for gauging DBP's liquidity. This agreement was reconfirmed during
negotiations. As to the share capital increase, the Government has taken
necessary legal actions (i.e., amendments to DBP's Charter to raise its
authorized capital in order to accommodate the scheduled increase in paid-in
capital) and has already raised DBP's share capital to P 3.2 billion. At
negotiations the Government confirmed to make an additional contribution
of P 825 million by 1983 raising the paid-in capital to the required level
of P 4.0 billion. The Government has actively assisted DBP in lining up
additional resources totalling P 6.4 billion in 1978-79 period. DBP sold,
or agreed to sell its large investments (P 2 billion) in three companies to
other government agencies on deferred terms spread over 18-40 years, free of



ANNEX 6
Page 10
-127 -
interest. The sale arrangements clearly do not serve DBP's interest and,
therefore, DBP has decided to exert its best efforts to renegotiate these sale
arrangements so that DBP may: (a) recover its legitimate claims (accumulated
interest and dividends) it had earlier agreed to forego; (b) be fully absolved
of all its future liabilities in respect of these projects; and (c) be paid a
realistic interest on the receivables during the period of deferment of
payment.
18.       With a view to resolving the problem of arrears on loans made at the
Government behest, the Government signed in 1979 with DBP a Guarantee Fund
Agreement whereby it assured DBP to provide liquidity support in case DBP's
overall liquidity position is imperilled and paid P 100 million as a compen-
sation against social loans with balance (P 440 million) to be paid in 8
yearly installments. The Government was unable to provide full cash compensa-
tion to DBP for arrears (totalling about P 1.1 billion) on behest projects
for two reasons. First, instead of providing direct compensation, the Govern-
ment provided liquidity support to DBP by continuing to maintain its large
deposits with DBP. The Government considers this arrangement more feasible
and also consistent with its tight budgetary position. Secondly, the
Government feared that DBP, assured of automatic compensation, may not exert
all the efforts needed to recover these loans. Whereas Government's concerns
are understandable and legitmate, a clear understanding between DBP and the
Government on principles and procedures relating to the Government behest
projects is necessary. Consequently, DBP and the Government have agreed to
set up a Committee which would decide which projects should be included in
the list of Government behest projects. DBP would prepare detailed semi-
annual reports on the status, prospects and problems of each of these pro-
jects. Moreover, DBP will maintain separate financial accounts for Government
behest projects and for each fiscal year will submit to the Government through
the proposed Committee, its financial statements showing separately financial
results of Government behest projects and its regular operations. If losses
are incurred by DBP as a result of implementing these projects thereby impair-
ing the financial viability and liquidity position of DBP, the Government, at
the recommendation of the proposed Committee, would provide financial assist-
ance to DBP.
19.       Financial Performance.  DBP-s net income rose from P 133 million
(4.1% return on equity) in 1978 to P 178 million (5.0% return on equity) in
1979 (Annex 6, T-5). DBP's profitability is to a large extent a function of
its loan collection performance because DBP, in accordance with its modified
cash basis accounting policy,/l is expected to report interest income on a
/1 This policy is followed by all Government institutions in the Philippines.



ANNEX 6
- 128 -                           Page 11
cash basis and interest expenses on an accrual basis./l The continued low
level of profitability, therefore, reflects mainly poor collection perfor-
mance. However, in judging DBP's financial performance, it is essential to
recognize that DBP is a Government-owned development-oriented institution and
that its profitability is not strictly comparable to private profit-oriented
institutions. Nonetheless, in order to reduce its excessive reliance on
Government funds and ensure its own financial viability, DBP should maximize
its earnings within the constraints of its developmental objectives. This
would require: (a) drastic measures to improve its collection performance
(para. 20); (b) a satisfactory resolution of the issue of Government behest
projects (para. 18); and (c) a review and revision of its peso lending rates.
DBP's current lending rates /2 for large industrial lending range between
16-18% p.a. depending on the type of collateral but irrespective of currencies
in which loans are denominated. DBP's foreign currency lending rates seem to
be on the higher side considering the fact that the foreign exchange risk is
passed on to the subborrowers. However, peso lending rates are not suffi-
ciently high to cover: (a) the cost of its marginal borrowing (currently
around 15-17% p.a.); (b) its overhead cost; (c) loss provision; and (d) a
reasonable return on equity. Moreover, DBP's peso lending rates have been
lower than the going market rates. This feature has impeded the active
participation of private financial institutions in long-term lending as they
have not been able to compete with DBP. DBP has started a review of its
lending rates based on the principle that its lending rates should, in
general, be competitive with the market rate and also consistent with the
Government's interest rate policy which emphasizes development of long-term
lending market. This review will be completed and rates adjusted before
December 31, 1981.
20.       Portfolio Quality.  DBP's loan portfolio totalling P 12.8 billion
at end 1979 remained reasonably well diversified among various sectors.
However, DBP's portfolio, on account of its broad sectoral coverage, remains
highly vulnerable to cyclical changes in business and economic conditions
particularly because the industrial sector has yet to overcome its structural
weaknesses. This vulnerability coupled with weaknesses in DBP-s appraisal
standards in the past and continuing weaknesses in its loan supervision
activities have resulted in unsatisfactorily high arrearages and extremely low
/1 However, DBP's interest income totalling P 1.17 billion reported for
1979 included P 607 million of "noncash collections". Had collection
of this nature been excluded from DBP's interest income, its net income
would have been negative. However, had DBP reported its interest income
on an accrual basis, accrued interest and fees totaling P 527 million
would have substantially offset negative impact of "non-cash collection"
on DBP's net income.
/2  DWPs standard interest rates on small loans range from 12% p.a.  (for
loans below P 150,000) to 16% p.a. (for loans above P 150,000 to
P 3 million). Loans under the Government's social development program,
however, carry interest of 9% p.a.

- 129 -
collections on DBP's loan portfolio /1 (Annex 6, T-6). In the past three
years, DBP has taken a number of steps to overcome its chronic arrears
problems. First, in order to help major industries overcome structural
weaknesses, DBP has identified the need for the formulation of appropriate
rationalization programs for two major industries (i.e., cement and textiles)
with active support from the Government and the Bank. Second, with a view to
rehabilitating projects with debt servicing problems, DBP has provided in
1978-79, massive rehabilitation assistance to its defaulting clients in the
form of refinancing and restructuring (paras. 12 and 13). Third, DBP has
tried, although not with complete success yet, to resolve the problem of
arrears on Government behest projects (para. 18). Fourth, DBP has intensified
the collection drive started in 1977. Finally, DBP set up in 1980, a new
department exclusively for the intensive supervision of its large problem
accounts. Most of these efforts were steps in the right direction but
obviously were not very effective. Therefore, DBP has agreed to adopt the
following additional measures to control the problem more effectively over the
long-term: (a) a comprehensive review of its portfolio of relatively large
projects where DBP's total exposure is $5 million or more and which have a
history of problems in the past two years, in order to determine the intrinsic
strength of its portfolio, and the magnitude and nature of assistance needed
by the projects in trouble. This review would be conducted by independent
consultants appointed in consultation with the Bank. On the basis of these
reviews, critical decision would be taken on these projects by DBP/Government;
(b) preparation and adoption of reaslistic cash collection targets for each
operating unit; (c) rigorous periodic monitoring of the cash collection
performance of each unit; (d) upgrading of quality of appraisal; (e) timely
legal action against willful defaulters; and (f) intensive supervision of
projects.
21.       DBP's long-term guarantee portfolio, which stood at P 3.2 billion
as of March 31, 1980, is characterized by heavy concentration in a few large
accounts. Five large accounts represented 60% of total guarantees. Arrears
relating to guarantees increased from 8% of total outstanding guarantees in
1977 to 20% as of March 31, 1980 despite the massive conversion of advances
under guarantees into loans or equity investments that have taken place in the
last two years. This development reflected the sizeable increase in
repayments on suppliers credits falling due in 1978 through 1980. In order
to arrest the increasing trend of arrears on guarantees, DBP has upgraded the
standard of its appraisal of guarantee requests through implementation of its
policy to offer a loan/guarantee package rather than a separate guarantee; but
the delinquency picture on guarantees is unlikely to change in the near future
as most of the guarantees in default had been approved before DBP decided to
/1 Arrearages rose from P 935 million (10.5% of total portfolio) as of
June 30, 1977 to P 1.5 billion (11.4%) as of December 31, 1979 and the
loan collection ratio deteriorated from 57% of total collectibles in
1977 to 33% in 1979.

adopt higher standards of appraisal. Reflecting the extensive conversion of
arrears on loans and guarantees into equity investments in 1978-79, DBP's
equity portfolio grew from P 939 million at the end of 1977 to P 2.36
billion as of March 31, 1980.  The realizable value of DBP-s equity portfolio
fell short by about P 424 million of the carrying value in DBP's book as the
result of conversion of arrearages (mostly interest in arrears) on unhealthy
accounts into equity investment./l DBP's acquired assets portfolio increased
from P 1.5 billion at the end of 1977 to P 1.7 billion as of March 31,
1980; possible losses on this portfolio seem to have been adequately provided
for.
22.       Reserves Policy and Position.  Provisions for doubtful accounts and
reserves for contingency, accumulated by DBP in accordance with its Statement
of Reserves Policy, stood at P 540 million (or 2.5% of total long-term
portfolio including guarantees) as of March 31, 1980; these provisions and
reserves, however, do not reflect doubts about the actual value of DBP's
equity investments as discussed in the preceeding paragraph and therefore are
not adequate. DBP and its auditors would undertake a thorough review of
DBP's portfolio (equity portfolio in particular) and in light of this review
DBP would set up appropriate reserves; an understanding to this effect was
reached at negotiations. DBP's reserves policy also requires DBP to appro-
priate 20% of its annual net income as the foreign exchange risk reserve; such
reserve stood at P 111 million or 15% of DBP's net exchange risk exposure /2
(the same percentage as required under its policy) as of March 31, 1980.
Audit
23.       DBP's accounts are audited by the Commission on Audit (COA), a
Government body. COA auditors have prepared long-form audit reports in line
with the Bank's "Illustrative Form of Audit for DFCs" since 1976. They have
achieved certain progress in upgrading the quality of their reports along the
lines suggested by a Bank-appointed audit expert in 1975. They now disclose
the effects of certain important deviations from generally accepted accouting
principles such as the modified cash basis accounting in their reports and
have gradually expanded coverage of portfolio review. However, there are
certain areas where further improvements can be made. COA auditors have
agreed to incorporate such improvements in their audit of DBP's 1980 accounts.
/1  However, as discussed in para. 19, the impact of this estimated over-
valuation on DBP's net worth would have been substantially offset by
additional interest income, had DBP adopted accrual basis accounting.
/2  The level of DBP's foreign exchange risk exposure declined from US$267
million as of December 31, 1977 to US$179 million as of March 31, 1980.
Of this $179 million, agreements have been finalized with respect to
$105 million for transferring exchange risk to its clients as disburse-
ments are made. Given this agreed transfer of risk, DBP's net exchange
risk exposure stood at US$74 million as of March 31, 1980.

Projected Operations and Finance
24.       Projected Operations and Resource Requirements.  DBP's projected
level of loan commitments over the next four years (1980-83) amounts to P
16.6 billion (about F 4 billion a year)/l with financing of capital aoods
imports representing P 5.9 billion (about $787 million) (Annex 6, T-7).  The
planned level of operations is markedly higher than the levels of previous
four years mainly because of the inclusion of DBP's special lending programs
(for rehabilitation of cement and textile industries, P 700 million /2; and
the expansion of gold mines, P 500 million /3) and the projected across-the-
board increases (15-20X p.a.) in lending to various sectors largely to reflect
the impact of inflation. While the state of DBP-s project pipeline supports
its expanded lending program, the availability of rcsources is likely to be a
constraint to realization of the program. Recognizing this possible
constraint, DBP is seriously considering syndicate operations for large
industrial loans. At the beginning of 1980, DBP had foreign currency
resources available for further commitments totalling $46 million (Annex 6,
T-8). In addition, $150 million /4 are being lined up to become available in
1981, a 10-year bond issue of Y 10 billion (about $42 million) on the Tokyo
capital market in 1981 is being arranged, an ADB's financing package of $60
million (including $30 million representing a cofinancing portion) is being
considered both by DBP and ADB, and about $100 million specifically for the
rehabilitation of cement and textile industries is being sought from the
Government. The balance (about $300 million) of DBP's foreign currency
resource requirements up to middle of 1983 is expected to be met from ADFU
funds and through additional borrowings from the Central Bank under the
Consolidated Foreign Borrowing Program /5.
/1 In the first nine months of 1980, DBP-s loan approvals (net of refinanc-
ing) reached P 3 billion to 11,678 clients including foreign currency
loans totalling US$207 million to 60 client companies.
/2 Tentative figure for planning purposes used by DBP. Actual requirements
may become higher.
/3 During the first nine months of 1980, DBP approved two loans totalling

/4 Government's foreign currency deposit of $45 million to be placed with

from U. S. Eximbank ($75 million) and from Japan Eximbank ($30 million).
/5 In 1980, DBP drew down $121 million under this program.

- 132 -                         Page 15
25.       DBP's peso requirements for its local currency loan disbursements
total P 10.8 billion over the next four years, part of which would be met by_
unused balances of Bank loans (P 420 million) for small and medium indus-
tries and agricultural projects, an ADB loan for fishery projects (P 64
million) and Central Bank fund for new hotel financing (P 160 million). DBP
expects its share capital to be raised at least by P 974 million and plans
to issue countryside bills totalling P 963 million in 1980-83. The balance
of its local currency requirements (P 8.2 billion) is expected to be met
from internally generated funds (P 1.4 million), net loan collections
(P 2.3 billion), advances from the Central Bank (P 1.0 million), addi-
tional deposits (P 2 billion) placed by the Government and by SSS and other
sources (P 700 million). In addition to funds required for its lending, DBP
expects to require P 1.6 billion in 1980-83 to meet advances on guarantees;
this requirement will be met largely by borrowings from the Central Bank under
the Consolidated Foreign Borrowing Program.
26.       Projected Financial Position and Performance.  Based on the above
operational and funding programs, DBP's total assets are projected to grow at
an average annual rate of 13% to P 40.1 billion at the end of 1984 (Annex 6,
T-9). A large part of the asset growth will be financed by debt; as a result,
the total debt/equity ratio is expected to rise from 5.9:1 as of March 31,
1980 to 6.5:1 in 1984. Therefore, DBP does not need any relaxation in its
present debt/equity limit of 10:1. DBP-s liquidity position is expected to
remain tight with current ratio being below unity throughout the projection
period despite projected improvements in collection rates which are by no
means certain./l However, the basic assumption underlying DBP-s financial
projections is that the Government would continue to provide liquidity support
to DBP; this was confirmed by the Government at negotiations. Providing
assumptions about collection rates are borne out by experience, DBP-s net
income is expected to rise gradually from P 178 million in 1979 to P 417
million (Annex 6, T-10); as a percentage of average total assets, however, net
income would remain at a stable level of around 1.2%. If expected levels of
collections are achieved, DBP's debt service cover would be above 1.2
throughout the projection period (Annex 6, T-11).
/1 Actual cash collection of principal installments amounted to P 793
million in 1979; the projected levels of collections (P 1.2 billion
in 1980, P 1.4 billion in 1981 and P 1.7 billion in 1982, and
P 2.0 billion in 1983) are, therefore, ambitious.

PHIILIPPINES
INDUSTRIAL FINANCE PROJECT
DEVELOPMENT BANK OF THE PHILIPPINES
Statement of Operating Policies and Procedures for Medium-
and Large-Scale Industrial Financing /1
1.        The policies, criteria and procedures set forth below will guide
DBPs medium- and large-scale industrial operations - particularly those
undertaken by Industrial Projects Department I and irrespective of the sources
of funds for financing these operations.
2.        Objectives of Industrial Financing.  To assist the development of
the country by providing financial and technical assistance for the estab-
lishment of new industries as well as for the balancing, modernization and
expansion of existing industries.
3.        Basis for Investment Decisions.  DBP will make investment decisions
on the basis of sound investment criteria and standards, and after careful
project evaluation. Financial assistance will be provided to those projects
which are financially and economically viable and technically feasible, and
for the implementation of which satisfactory organizational and managerial
arrangements have been made.
4.        Method of Financing.  DBP will provide finance according to the
requirements of each project, in one or several of the following forms:
(a) direct loans in local currency, for financing local expenditures
on fixed assets and where appropriate for working capital;
(b) direct loans in foreign currencies, for financing imports of
equipment and/or new materials;
(c) guarantees of domestic currency and/or foreign currency credits
made on suitable terms;
(d)  loans fully or partly convertible into stock;
(e) in exceptional and highly meritorious cases, direct equity
investments and underwriting of share issues.
/1 Approved by the Board of Governors on May 29, 1974, under Resolution
No. 1571-A.



ANNEX 6
134 -                       Attachment 1
Page 2
General Investment Policies
(a) Financing will be provided to projects which are in line with
the investment priorities of the Government; preference being
given to projects registered with the Board of Investments (BOI)
under the Invest-ment Incentives Act or the Export Incentives
Act, or with other pertinent Government regulatory agencies.
(b) In accordance with normal banking practice, adequate security
will be required to cover the loans and guarantees, but the
main considerations will be the merits of proposed projects,
their repayment prospects and the soundness of management
of the enterprise.
(c) DBP will not seek a controlling interest in any enterprise in
which it has invested, or any other interest which would give
it responsibility for management, except when in its judgment
its investment is in jeopardy, in which case DBP reserves the
option of taking such action as may be necessary to protect its
interests.
6.        Project Appraisal Guidelines
(a) Each investment decision will be made on the basis of a thorough
project evaluation, reflected in an appraisal report, which will be
a self-contained and comprehensive document outlining facts,
analyses and judgments on the essential aspects (including tech-
nical, marketing, management, financial and economic) of the project
as well as recommendations on special contractual arrangements. In
appropriate instances, the report will incorporate essential comple-
mentary elements of the BOI s appraisal work, in particular its
economic evaluation and marketing analysis. These inclusions
notwithstanding, DBP-s appraisal reports will in any event include
DBP's own judgments on these matters as well.
(b) Project appraisal will generally include the calculation of the
internal, financial and economic rates of return for projects
of which the total project cost exceeds US$2 million equivalent.
If the economic rate of return is below 10%, special justification
will be required in the appraisal report which recommends a
decision to finance the project.
(c)  Each appraisal report will provide in detail the estimated cost
of the project, including working capital requirements and
adequate contingencies. Prior to committing its own funds DBP
will satisfy itself that funds expected from sources other than
DBP (i.e., borrower's own contribution, plus borrowings from



ANNEX 6
Attachment I
- 135-                            Page 3
other lending institutions) will be available in sufficient
amounts when needed to cover the total financing requirements
of the project.
7.        Lending Terms
(a) Each industrial loan will be made on a term and with a grace
period consistent with the repayment capacity of the enterprise
based on projected cash flows, and related to the useful life
of the assets financed by the loan.
(b) Each investment project financed by DBP will normally have
an equity capital equivalent to 25% of total project cost. In the
case of an expansion project of an existing enterprise, funds
generated internationally are considered as equity for this
purpose.
(c) DBP will take steps adequate to protect itself against the
foreign exchange risk on its lending operations and guarantees.
8.        Project Supervision Guidelines.  It is the policy of DBP to:
(a) undertake systematic follow-up on the progress and operation of the
projects financed by it, in order to establish whether the actual develop-
ment correspond with the expectations and projections on the basis of which
the investment decision was made, and to keep DBP's management currently
informed of the project status so that it may take timely and appropriate
action. Accordingly, DBP requires its borrowers to submit regular period-
ical reports on the physical and financial progress of the project, and on
its operational and financial performance. Furthermore, projects are
visited at appropriate intervals to confirm the state of progress, to check
on the efficiency, organization and management of the project, and to discuss
problems of mutual concern with the borrower's management; and (b) maintain
close scrutiny of its accounts in arrears. Each individual account in
arrears is analyzed in detail at least once annually in order to identify
the causes for arrears and to determine the repayment capacity of the
enterprise. On the basis of this analysis, DBP's management takes appropriate
measures (including legal action), for collection, rescheduling, or
restructuring of the account.
AEPID
January 28, 1981

PHILIPPINES
INDUSTRIAL FINANCE PROJECT
DEVELOPMENT BANK OF THE PHILIPPINES
Program of Action for Improvements
1.        Outlined below are certain programs that the management of DBP has
initiated and will carry out in respect of its operations. Most of these
programs are the results of DBP's annual review of its past operations and
have been adopted after extensive discussions among members of the DBP Board
and the executive staff; others, specifically those relating to policy
considerations, are responses to national economic strategy and monetary
policy changes.
Review of Portfolio
2.        DBP has initiated a program that will review comprehensively DBP's
portfolio of relatively large projects (with total exposure of US$5 million
or more and having a history of problems in the past 2 years) to determine
the intrinsic strength of the portfolio and the magnitude and nature of
assistance needed by the projects in trouble and to draw lessons from past
experience. DBP has in fact secured a $1 million fund for technical
assistance from the structural adjustment loan which it has earmarked to
finance consulting services where required. DBP assigns high priority to
this portfolio review and hopes to complete by September 30, 1981 the initial
phase of the program including identification of the projects and establish-
ment of the timetable. DBP will pursue this program in close coordination
with ADFU and will keep it informed of further developments.
Collection Efforts
3.        DBP is presently reviewing its collection program and will
intensify its collection efforts thru means which will include (a)
preparation of realistic collection targets that will focus on cash
collections rather than on a combination of cash and noncash collections;
(b) close monitoring of such cash collections; and (c) more effective project
supervision including board and management participation, to improve
collection rates. DBP has already initiated closer monitoring and tightened
its reporting procedures on collections. A Collection Committee with DBP
Chairman as its Chairman and all operating departments as its members meet
every week to monitor the progress of collections.  Collection targets for
1981 have been set but will be modified to reflect the emphasis on cash
collections.



- 137 -                        ANI<-X 6
ATTACHMENT 2
Page 2
Sale of Three Large Investments
4.        In line with DBP's program of divesting itself of its substantial
investments in projects that are presently non-earning, DBP concluded the
sale of its investments in three large projects, namely, Food Terminal, Inc.
National Housing Corporation and National Steel Corporation.  The terms of
the sale of DBP investments in these projects were so designed in order
to enable DBP to recoup its principal investments over a period of time.
DBP realizes the income being foregone on the investment over the collection
period agreed upon but considers the transfer of the responsibility for
these projects (and the consequent further financial claims upon DBP) to be
the more significant consideration. Nonetheless, assuming that any or all of
these considerations can be achieved, DBP will attempt, on a best efforts
basis, to renegotiate the agreements or arrangements for the sale of
investments (equity, advances and acquired assets) in Food Terminal
Incorporated, National Housing Corporation, National Steel Corporation
including properties of the former Iligan Integrated Steel Mills so that DBP
may (a) shorten the period of repayment; (b) recover its claims (accumulated
interest and dividends) it had earlier agreed to forego; (c) be fully
absolved or compensated for all future liabilities in respect of these
projects; and (d) be paid a realistic interest on the receivables during the
period that the repayment is deferred. DBP will make provision for losses on
these accounts to the extent of defaults on them.
Review of Lending Rates
5.        In view of the recent changes adopted by the Monetary Board of the
Central Bank relative to interest rates which are to take effect in July
1981. DBP is now in the process of reviewing its own interest rate policies.
The new policies that are being considered by DBP will conform to the basic
principles that DBP's lending rates shall be generally:  (a) competitive with
market interest rates; and (b) consistent with government-s interest rate
policy. DBP will monitor the effects of the new Central Bank policies on
existing deposit and lending rates, will review and if found necessary will
adopt specific interest rate changes by December 31, 1981.
Policies on Refinancing
6.        DBP recognizes that there is a need to formulate and adopt policies
governing its operations on refinancing, conversion of arrearages into equity
investments and "dacion en pago . It has in fact rationalized these
operations by ensuring that only deserving projects are provided assistance
by undertaking a thorough reappraisal of project viability before refinancing,
conversion or dacion en pago is resorted to. DBP is presently reviewing its
policies and expects to adopt new ones by June 30, 1981.



-138-                            ANNEX 6
ATTACHMENT 2
Page 3
Organization of IPD I
7.        DBP believes that IPD I's organization can be made more responsive
by modifying its basic structure and staffing pattern.  Present thinking
envisions an organization that will be basically grouped according to
industry specialization, such groups (three) to be functionally integrated to
handle both project appraisal and project supervision. The Accounts
Servicing Group is expected to remain and will perform administrative and
bookkeeping functions. This will be finalized on or before June 30, 1981.
Provisions
8.        DBP and its auditors will undertake a thorough review of DBP's
portfolio (equity portfolio in particular) with a view to making appropriate
provisions for portfolio losses in the 1981 accounts.
Government Behest Projects
9.        Projects initiated at the instance of Government are defined as
those which the Governmelnt considers to be of national importance and for
which DBP is requested to finance even though such projects do not meet DBP-s
normal investment criteria. To ensure timely identification of such projects
and their subsequent satisfactory handling, the Government and DBP will set
up a high-level Committee which would determine on a case-to-case basis
whether a given project falls into that category. Decision by the Committee
will be taken on the basis of detailed evaluation reports on the projects
prepared and submitted to the Committee by DBP. DBP would prepare comprehen-
sive semi-annual progress reports on Government initiated projects (already
approved and those that may be approved in the future excluding those covered
by category "B" of the Guarantee Fund Agreement). These progress reports
will bring out the status and prospects of each project and the account
status. Moreover, DBP will maintain separate financial accounts (balance
sheet, cash-flow and profit and loss statements) for Government initiated
projects in accordance with its normal accounting policies and standards,
and for each fiscal year will submit to the Government through the Committee,
its financial statements showing separately financial results of Government
initiated projects and its regular operations. If losses are incurred by DBP
as a result of implementing these projects thereby impairing the financial
viability and liquidity position of DBP, the Government at the recommendation
of the Committee provide financial assistance to DBP.
AEPID
March 31, 1981

/a  Includes loan approvals for the rehabilitation of cement and textile industries (P 700 million) and for
the expansion of gold mines (P 500 million).
Assumptions
Approvals      - The projected loan approvals for 1980 are based on the actual results in the first quarter of
1980 and the pipeline of projects as of March 31, 1980, which included the rehabilitation pro-
gram of cement and textile industries and the expansion program of gold mines. The projected
decline in loan approvals in 1981 reflects the absence of special lending programs undertaken
in 1980. Approvals for 1982 through 1984 are based on the expected growth potential of each
sector.
Commitments   - Based on DBP-s past experience, 75% of loan approvals are assumed to be committed in the same
year and the remaining 25% in the following year.
Disbursements - are based on the closing dates of the respective credit lines, the nature and requirements of
projects and past pattern of DBP's disbursements.
AEPID
January 28, 1981
